8 min
Defendant Claims Property as Trust/Pawn/Usurpation from Absentee
Ruling on defendant claiming property as trust/pawn/usurpation from absentee

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when a defendant's claim of possession for an absentee dismisses the lawsuit, and identify the required evidence and conditions.

Core Idea
A defendant who disclaims ownership and proves possession for an identifiable absentee escapes litigation.

In lawsuits over disputed property, the key question is whether the defendant asserts ownership. This passage from Kitab al-Da'wa explains that if the defendant claims to hold the property as a trust, pawn, or usurpation from an absent person, and provides legal evidence, he is no longer a litigant. The plaintiff must then pursue the absentee. Understanding this ruling prevents procedural abuse and ensures the real owner is brought to account.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defendant's claim type
The defendant must claim one of three specific relationships: trust (amanah), pawn (rahn), or usurpation (ghasb) from an absent third party.
These are the only three claims recognized in this ruling; other claims may not suffice.
2
Confirm the defendant disclaims ownership
The defendant must explicitly not claim ownership of the property. If he claims ownership, he becomes a proper litigant, as in the al-Kindi hadith.
This is the fundamental distinction: ownership claim vs. possessory claim for another.
3
Requirement of evidence (bayyinah)
The defendant must present legal evidence (typically two male witnesses or one male and two females) to support his claim. Mere statement is insufficient.
This prevents fraudulent evasion by defendants who simply disclaim ownership without proof.
4
Requirement to identify the absent owner
The defendant must name the specific absentee and provide enough detail for the plaintiff to identify and sue the real owner.
Without identification, the plaintiff cannot pursue the absentee, and the defendant remains a litigant.
5
Contrast with the al-Kindi hadith
In the hadith of Hadrami, al-Kindi claimed ownership ('This land is mine'), making him a defendant. This shows that claiming ownership makes one a proper litigant, whereas disclaiming ownership with proof removes one from litigation.
This contrast clarifies the scope of the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[2766] (37) وان قال المدعى عليه هذا الشيء اودعنيه فلان الغائب او رهنه عندی او غصبته منه واقام بينة على ذلك فلا خصومة بينه وبين المدعى [2766] (37) إذا قال المدعي عليه: هذا الشيء أودعنيه فلان الغائب أو رهنه عندي أو غصبته منه وأقام بينة على ذلك فلا خصومة بينه وبين المدعي. اگر مدعی علیہ نے کہا یہ چیز میرے پاس امانت رکھی ہے فلاں غائب نے یا اس نے اس کو رہن رکھی ہے میرے پاس یا میں نے اس کو اس سے غصب کیا ہے۔ اور ان پر مبینہ قائم کیا تو اس کے درمیان اور مدعی کے درمیان جھگڑا نہیں رہے گا۔ ان تینوں مسئلوں میں مدعی علیہ یہ کہہ ہی نہیں رہا ہے کہ یہ چیز میری ہے بلکہ بینہ قائم کر کے کہہ رہا ہے کہ یہ چیز فلاں کی ہے میرے پاس تو امانت یا رہن کی ہے اس لئے فلاں کے پاس جاؤ۔ وہ جب کہے گا میں آپ کو دے دوں گا۔ اس لئے مدعی علیہ خصم اور جھگڑا کرنے والا نہیں رہے گا۔ البتہ ان تینوں باتوں پر بینہ قائم کرنا ہوگا اور اصل مالک کا پتا دینا ہوگا کہ یہ چیز مثلا زید نے میرے پاس امانت رکھی ہے، یا زید نے میرے پاس رہن رکھی ہے، یا زید سے اس کو میں نے غصب کیا ہے۔ تا کہ کہیں ایسا نہ ہو کہ مدعی علیہ اپنی جان چھڑارہا ہو اور خصم بننے سے چکما دے کر جان بچارہا ہو۔ اس لئے ان باتوں پر بینہ بھی قائم کرنا ہوگا۔ اور اصل مالک کا بھی پتا دینا ہوگا (۲) حضرت حضرمی والی حدیث میں کندی نے کہا تھا کہ یہ زمین میری ہے تب وہ مدعی علیہ بنا تھا۔ حدیث کی عبارت یہ ہے۔ فقال الکندی می ارضى فى يدى ازرعها ليس له فيها حق (الف) (ابوداؤد شریف، باب الرجل يخلف على علمه فیما غاب عند ص ۱۵۴ نمبر ۳۶۲۳) اس حدیث میں کندی نے دعوی کیا کہ یہ زمین میری ہے تب وہ مدعی علیہ اور مقصم بنے تھے۔ اور یوں کہتے کہ یہ چیز میری نہیں ہے دوسرے نے میرے پاس امانت رکھی ہے تو وہ خصم نہیں بنتے۔ حاشیہ : (الف) کندی نے کہا یہ میری زمین ہے میرے قبضے میں ہے۔ میں اس میں ہوتا ہوں اس میں اس کا حق نہیں ہے۔
And if the defendant says: 'This thing was deposited with me by so-and-so who is absent, or he pawned it with me, or I usurped it from him,' and he provides evidence for that, then there is no lawsuit between him and the plaintiff. [Commentary: If the defendant says: 'This thing is a trust with me from such absent person, or he has pawned it with me, or I have usurped it from him,' and he provides evidence for that, then there will be no dispute between him and the plaintiff. In these three issues, the defendant is not saying that this thing is mine; rather, he is providing evidence that it belongs to someone else and is with him as a trust or pawn, so go to that person. When that person will say, 'I will give it to you' — therefore, the defendant will not remain a defendant or litigant. However, he must provide evidence for these three matters and also indicate the original owner, e.g., 'Zaid deposited this with me,' or 'Zaid pawned it with me,' or 'I usurped it from Zaid.' This is so that the defendant is not just trying to evade and escape from being a litigant. So he must provide evidence for these matters and also indicate the original owner. (2) In the hadith of Hadrami, al-Kindi said: 'This land is mine.' Then he became the defendant. The text of the hadith is: 'Al-Kindi said: This is my land, in my hand, I cultivate it; he has no right in it.' (Abu Dawood, Bab al-Rajul Yukhlifu 'Ala 'Ilmihi Fima Ghaba 'Inda, p. 154, no. 3623). In this hadith, al-Kindi claimed that the land was his, so he became the defendant and the object of the claim. If he had said, 'This thing is not mine; someone else deposited it with me,' then he would not be a litigant. Footnote: (a) Al-Kindi said: 'This is my land, in my possession, I cultivate it; he has no right in it.'
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from Kitab al-Da'wa (The Book of Claims) in Hanafi fiqh. It states that if the defendant claims that the disputed property is held by him as a trust (amanah), pawn (rahn), or usurped property (ghasb) from an absent third party, and he provides evidence (bayyinah) for this, then the lawsuit between the plaintiff and this defendant is dismissed. The defendant is not considered a proper litigant because he is not asserting any right of ownership over the property; he merely acknowledges possession for another. However, he must produce evidence and identify the absent owner to prevent fraudulent evasion. The commentary contrasts this with the well-known hadith of al-Kindi, where al-Kindi claimed ownership (saying 'this land is mine'), making him a defendant. The footnote explains al-Kindi's exact words. This ruling underscores that the key factor in determining whether a person is a valid litigant is whether he claims ownership of the property in dispute.

Key Terms
خصم (khasm): A party to the lawsuit, specifically the defendant who is subject to the claim.
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically two male witnesses or one male and two females, presented before the judge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, if the defendant claims the disputed property is a trust from an absent person, what must he provide to avoid being a litigant?
A) A written document only
B) A sworn oath
C) Legal evidence (bayyinah) and identification of the owner
D) A single witness

Key Takeaway
A defendant who disclaims ownership and proves possession for an identifiable absentee is not a litigant, preventing procedural evasion and ensuring the real owner is brought to account.

Claim of Purchase from Absentee Establishes Litigant Status
Ruling that defendant's claim of purchase is an ownership claim, making him a litigant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling that a defendant claiming purchase from an absentee remains a litigant and explain the qiyas basis.

Core Idea
Claiming purchase asserts ownership, making the defendant a proper litigant.

In litigation, the identity of the defendant matters. This passage addresses a common tactic: a defendant claims to have bought the disputed property from an absentee, hoping to escape the lawsuit. The Hanafi ruling clarifies that such a claim actually confirms the defendant's stake, keeping them in the case. The commentary links this to the hadith of al-Kindi, establishing a clear qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Read the matn: 'And if he says: I bought it from so-and-so who is absent, then he is a litigant.' Note that the ruling is unconditional: the defendant remains a party to the suit.
2
Understand the Legal Reason
The commentary explains that claiming purchase implies ownership ('this is my ownership'). Ownership is the basis for being a defendant in a property dispute.
The implicit ownership is the operative cause (illa).
3
Trace the Qiyas
The author analogizes to the hadith of al-Kindi, who claimed ownership and was treated as a litigant. Here, the defendant claims purchase, which is a form of ownership claim. By qiyas jaliy, the same ruling applies.
The asl is al-Kindi's case; the far' is the current scenario; the illa is 'assertion of ownership'.
4
Apply the Ruling
In practice, the judge will proceed with the case against this defendant. He cannot shift the suit to the absentee seller. All proceedings continue as normal.
This prevents frivolous avoidance of litigation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[2767] (38) وان قال ابتعته من فلان الغائب فهو خصم [2767] (38) وإذا قال: ابتعته من فلان الغائب فهو خصم. اور اگر کہا کہ میں اس کو فلاں سے خریدا ہے تو وہ خصم ہو گا۔ جب کہا میں نے اس کو فلاں سے خریدا ہے تو یہ بھی کہ رہا ہے کہ یہ میری ملکیت ہے۔ اور جب ملکیت کا دعوی کر رہا ہے تو وہ مدعی علیہ اور خصم بنے گا۔ اب مقدمے کی ساری کاروائیاں اس کے خلاف چلیں گی۔ اوپر کی حدیث میں کندی نے ملکیت کا دعوی کیا تو وہ خصم ہوئے اور مقدے کی ساری کاروائیاں اسی کے خلاف ہوئیں۔
And if he says: 'I bought it from so-and-so who is absent,' then he is a litigant. [Commentary:] If he says, 'I bought it from so-and-so,' then he is also saying that this is my ownership. And when he claims ownership, he becomes a defendant and litigant. Now all proceedings of the case will be conducted against him. In the above hadith, al-Kindi claimed ownership, so he became a litigant, and all proceedings of the case were conducted against him.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is the second ruling in the series. It states that if the defendant says 'I bought this property from an absent person,' then he remains a litigant (خصم). The reason is that by claiming purchase, he implicitly asserts ownership of the property. Since ownership is the basis of a valid defense in a claim, he is now a proper defendant. The commentary explicitly connects this to the hadith of al-Kindi mentioned earlier: because al-Kindi claimed ownership (saying 'this land is mine'), he was treated as a defendant. Analogously, a claim of purchase is a claim of ownership, so the defendant cannot escape the lawsuit.

Key Terms
ابتیاع (ibtiya'): Purchase; a contract of sale transferring ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the defendant remain a litigant when he claims to have purchased the property from an absent third party?
A) Because the purchase is invalid
B) Because the purchase makes him the owner
C) Because he is a guarantor for the seller
D) Because he must produce the seller

Key Takeaway
A defendant who claims purchase from an absentee remains a litigant because the claim inherently asserts ownership, which is the basis for being a party in a property dispute.

Plaintiff Claims Theft but Defendant Claims Trust
Ruling on conflicting claims of theft and trust

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a plaintiff proves theft and the defendant proves trust, and explain why the lawsuit remains pending.

Core Idea
Conflicting evidence does not dismiss a lawsuit; the defendant's trust claim fails to remove him from the case.

In judicial proceedings, conflicting claims supported by evidence require careful judicial analysis. This lesson examines a specific scenario where a plaintiff claims theft and proves it, while the possessor claims the property was deposited with him by an absent person and also proves it. The Hanafi ruling clarifies that the defendant remains a litigant because the claims are contradictory regarding the origin of possession, and the trust claim does not negate the theft allegation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claims
The plaintiff claims theft (sariqa); the defendant claims trust (wadi'ah) from an absent person.
Both claims are about the origin of possession.
2
Note the evidence
Both parties have established proof (bayyinah) for their respective claims.
3
State the outcome
The lawsuit is not dismissed (لم تندفع الخصومة); the defendant remains a party.
Despite having evidence of trust, the defendant does not escape litigation.
4
Explain the rationale
The defendant's claim of trust does not directly counter the plaintiff's theft claim; it merely explains how he came into possession. The plaintiff's theft claim raises suspicion that the trust might be a cover, so the case must proceed.
Contrast with scenarios where the defendant claims ownership of the property for an absentee, which may end the lawsuit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[2768] (39) وان قال المدعى سرق منى واقام البينة وقال صاحب اليد اودعنيه فلان واقام البينة لم تندفع الخصومة. [2768] (39) وإذا قال المدعي: سرق مني وأقام البينة، وقال صاحب اليد: أودعنيه فلان وأقام البينة، لم تندفع الخصومة. اگر مدعی نے کہا کہ میری چیز چرائی گئی ہے اور بینہ قائم کیا۔ اور قبضے والے نے کہا نمیرے پاس فلاں نے امانت رکھی ہے اور بینہ قائم کیا تو خصومت ختم نہیں ہوگی۔ مدعی دعوی کر رہا ہے کہ میری چیز چرائی گئی ہے اور چرانے پر بینہ قائم کیا۔ اور جس کے قبضے میں چیز ہے وہ دعوی کرتا ہے کہ میرے پاس فلاں نے امانت رکھی ہے اور امانت رکھنے پر بینہ قائم کیا تو مدعی علیہ سے خصومت ختم نہیں ہوگی۔
And if the plaintiff says: 'It was stolen from me,' and provides evidence, and the person in possession says: 'So-and-so deposited it with me,' and provides evidence, the lawsuit is not dismissed. [Commentary:] If the plaintiff says: 'My property was stolen' and provides evidence, and the possessor says: 'So-and-so deposited it with me' and provides evidence, then the litigation will not end. The plaintiff is claiming that his property was stolen and provides evidence of theft. The one in possession claims that someone deposited it with him and provides evidence of deposit. Therefore, the lawsuit against the defendant is not dismissed.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This is the third ruling, dealing with a conflict where the plaintiff claims theft (sariqa) and proves it, while the possessor claims that the property was deposited with him by a third party (absentee) and proves that. In this scenario, the defendant's claim of trust does not remove him from being a litigant. The reason, as understood from the commentary, is that the plaintiff's claim of theft directly implicates the possession as possibly stolen, and the mere claim of trust does not negate the plaintiff's rights. The defendant must either establish his own valid title or return the property. Unlike the cases in ruling 2766, here the defendant does not claim that the property belongs to the absent person in a way that absolves him; rather, he claims possession via trust, but the plaintiff's theft claim raises a suspicion that the trust might be a cover. Thus, the lawsuit proceeds against the possessor.

Key Terms
سرق (saraqa): Theft; taking property secretly without right, with a minimum threshold (nisab) for amputation penalty.
صاحب اليد (sahib al-yad): The person in physical possession of the property, the defendant in this case.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, if the plaintiff proves theft and the possessor proves a trust from an absent person, what happens to the lawsuit?
A) Dismissed immediately
B) Defendant remains a litigant
C) Plaintiff must provide more evidence
D) The absent person must be summoned first

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when conflicting proofs are presented, the lawsuit against the possessor continues if the plaintiff proves theft and the defendant proves trust, because the trust claim does not negate the theft claim.

Oath in Purchase Claims: Current State vs Past Act
Ruling with proof — talil: oath wording to prevent tawriya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the wording of an oath in a claim of purchase and distinguish between Abu Hanifa’s and Abu Yusuf’s positions, explaining the rationale of blocking tawriya.

Core Idea
In purchase claims, the oath is on the current state, not the past act — to block evasion through tawriya.

When a seller denies a claimed sale, the judge must administer an oath. But what words? If we ask 'Did you sell?' the defendant might use tawriya — for example, if the sale was later rescinded, he can truthfully say 'I did not sell' (meaning now). To prevent this, Imam Abu Hanifa ruled the oath must be on the current non-existence of the sale. This lesson unpacks that ruling and the intra-madhhab difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario
A buyer claims he purchased an item from the seller. The seller denies the sale. The judge must administer an oath to the defendant (seller).
The ruling applies to denials of purchase claims specifically.
2
Understand the two possible oath wordings
Option 1: Oath on the past act — 'I did not sell.' Option 2: Oath on the current state — 'There is no existing sale between you in it.'
The wording determines the defendant's ability to evade.
3
Grasp the problem of tawriya
Tawriya is using ambiguous wording to give a false impression while technically telling the truth. If the oath is on the past act, a defendant who had a sale but later rescinded it could swear 'I did not sell' meaning 'not now,' misleading the court.
Blocking tawriya is the key rationale (talil) for the ruling.
4
Learn Abu Hanifa's position
Imam Abu Hanifa ruled the oath must be on the current state: 'There is no existing sale between you in it.' This prevents the defendant from exploiting the past-tense wording.
This is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position.
5
Contrast with Abu Yusuf's position
Imam Abu Yusuf held the oath should be on the past act: 'I did not sell.' His reasoning: the claim is about a past event. The text notes this ikhtilaf.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf): under Abu Yusuf's wording, a defendant who rescinded a sale could evade honestly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۷۷۵] (۴۶) ومن ادعى انه ابتاع من هذا عبده بالف فجحده استحلف بالله ما بينكما بيع قائم فيه ولا يستحلف بالله بالله ما بعت
And whoever claims that he bought from this person his slave for a thousand and he (the seller) denies it, he (the defendant) is made to swear by Allah: 'There is no existing sale between you in it.' And he is not made to swear by Allah: 'I did not sell.'
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage states a ruling on oath-taking in a claim of purchase. The defendant (seller) denies the sale. The judge administers the oath on the current state (no existing sale now) rather than on the past act of selling. The reason is to prevent tawriya (evasion through verbal trickery): if the sale was later rescinded, the defendant could truthfully swear 'I did not sell' (meaning now), but the oath on the current state blocks that. This follows Imam Abu Hanifa's principle, which differs from Imam Abu Yusuf who would administer the oath on the past act.

Key Terms
الیمین (al-yamin): The oath sworn by the defendant in a lawsuit.
التوریة (al-tawriya): Using ambiguous wording to give a false impression while technically telling the truth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a claim of purchase where the defendant denies, how does Imam Abu Hanifa rule the oath should be administered?
A) 'I did not sell it.'
B) 'There is no existing sale between you in it.'
C) 'I do not owe you anything.'
D) 'By Allah, I am truthful.'

Key Takeaway
In a purchase claim denial, the Hanafi mufta bihi is to administer the oath on the current non-existence of the sale, not on the past act of selling, to prevent tawriya.

Oath in Claim of Usurpation: On Current Liability Not Past Act
Ruling on oath wording to prevent tawriya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the correct wording of the oath in a claim of usurpation (ghasb) according to Imam Abu Hanifa and explain why the oath is on current liability rather than the past act.

Core Idea
In ghasb claims, the defendant swears on the current absence of liability, not on the past act, to block tawriya.

This passage parallels the previous ruling on debt but for usurpation (ghasb). The key ruling is that the defendant swears about the present right to restitution, not about whether the usurpation occurred. This is based on Imam Abu Hanifa's principle to block tawriya (deceptive evasion). By swearing on current liability, a defendant who usurped and later returned the item cannot truthfully deny the claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is administering an oath to a defendant in a claim of usurpation. The ruling is that the judge must make him swear using the wording: 'By Allah, you do not have the right to demand the return of this specific item nor the return of its value.'
This is a procedural ruling for qada.
2
Contrast the two wordings
The prohibited wording is 'By Allah, I did not usurp.' The preferred wording focuses on the current right of the plaintiff, not the past act. Understand the difference and its legal effect.
The past-act wording is what Imam Abu Yusuf would use.
3
Analyze the rationale (talil)
The reason (talil) is to block tawriya. If the defendant actually usurped the item but later returned it, he could truthfully swear 'I did not usurp' meaning 'I am not currently usurping.' But he cannot truthfully swear that the plaintiff has no current right to restitution because after returning, the plaintiff indeed has no claim. Thus the current-liability oath prevents evasion.
Tawriya is a statement with a hidden meaning used to mislead.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Abu Yusuf disagreed and would administer the oath on the past act. Identify his reasoning: he viewed the claim as about the past act of usurpation, and the oath should directly address that. But the preferred position is that of Imam Abu Hanifa.
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The author of Al-Sharh al-Sameri prefers Imam Abu Hanifa's view. The ruling is drawn from zahir al-riwayah. For fatwa, the current-liability wording is used.
This is a clear ruling with a well-known tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۷۷۶] (۴۷) ويستحلف في الغصب بالله ما يستحق عليك رد هذه العين ولا رد قيمتها ولا يستحلف بالله ما غصبت.
And he is made to swear in a claim of usurpation by Allah: 'You do not have the right to demand the return of this specific item nor the return of its value.' And he is not made to swear by Allah: 'I did not usurp.'
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage parallels the previous one but for claims of usurpation (ghasb). The defendant swears that the plaintiff has no current right to restitution of the item or its value. The oath is not on the past act of usurpation. The reason is again to block tawriya: if the defendant usurped but later returned the item, he could truthfully swear 'I did not usurp' (meaning now) and evade liability. By swearing on the current lack of obligation, he cannot escape. This again follows Imam Abu Hanifa's principle. Imam Abu Yusuf, by contrast, would administer the oath on the past act.

Key Terms
الغصب (al-ghasb): Taking another's property without right and without khilaf (forcibly).
التورية (al-tawriya): A statement with a hidden meaning intended to deceive; in legal context, swearing truthfully but misleadingly to avoid liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school (Imam Abu Hanifa's view), what is the correct wording of the oath for a defendant in a claim of usurpation?
A) 'By Allah, I did not usurp.'
B) 'By Allah, you have no right to demand the return of this item or its value.'
C) 'By Allah, I am not a usurper.'
D) 'By Allah, I am innocent of this claim.'

Key Takeaway
The oath in ghasb claims focuses on present liability, not the past act, to block tawriya.

Oath in Claims of Nikah and Talaq: Current Status, Except for 'Itaq
Rulings on oath wording in nikah and talaq claims, with exception for emancipation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the correct oath formulation for claims of nikah and talaq, explain the reasoning (talil) behind the current status rule, and identify the exception for emancipation.

Core Idea
In marriage and divorce claims, oath is on present status, not past act — except for irrevocable emancipation.

When a marriage or divorce is claimed, the defendant's oath must be carefully worded to prevent deception. Imam Abu Hanifa directs the judge to administer an oath on the current marital status, not on the past act. This lesson explains the specific formulations and the rational basis, along with an important exception for slave emancipation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claims and oath formulas
Read the source text to note the two separate rules: for nikah claim, the oath is 'By Allah, there is no marriage between you at present.' For talaq claim, the oath is 'By Allah, she is not irrevocably divorced from you at this hour.'
The text explicitly gives the Arabic wording.
2
Understand the prohibition on past act oath
The text states: 'He is not made to swear: I did not divorce her.' This shows that an oath on the past act (e.g., 'I did not divorce') is impermissible. Identify the reason: the defendant could have divorced and remarried, then truthfully swear 'I did not divorce' meaning currently.
This is the talil (reasoning) based on preventing tawriya (deception).
3
Analyze the exception for 'Itaq
Note that emancipation ('itaq) is irrevocable. Therefore, the risk of tawriya does not exist; the oath can be on the past act. This exception is mentioned in the commentary, not the base text, but is crucial.
Emancipation cannot be undone, so no possibility of restoration.
4
Locate the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Abu Yusuf held that the oath should always be on the past act, regardless of the claim. Contrast his view with the majority position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
This ikhtilaf affects judicial practice.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the current status oath for nikah and talaq. For 'itaq, the oath on the past act is accepted due to irrevocability.
Authority: Zahir al-Riwayah and later Hanafi jurists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۷۷۷] (۴۸) وفي النكاح بالله ما بينكما نكاح قائم فى الحال وفي دعوى الطلاق بالله ماهی بائن منك الساعة بما ذكرت ولا يستحلف بالله ماطلقها
And in marriage: 'By Allah, there is no marriage existing between you at the present time.' And in a claim of divorce: 'By Allah, she is not irrevocably separated from you at this hour regarding what you mentioned.' And he is not made to swear by Allah: 'I did not divorce her.'
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage extends the same principle to claims of marriage and divorce. For a marriage claim, the defendant swears that no marriage currently exists. For a divorce claim, the defendant swears that the woman is not currently irrevocably divorced (bain) due to the claimed divorce. The oath is not on the past act of divorce. The reason is similar: if the husband divorced and then remarried, he could swear 'I did not divorce' meaning now. The oath on current status prevents this. However, there is an exception for emancipation ('itaq): because emancipation is irrevocable, the defendant cannot later restore the slave's status, so the oath can be on the past act. This exception is noted in the commentary.

Key Terms
البائن (al-bā'in): The woman who is irrevocably divorced; her marriage bond is completely severed.
العتاق (al-'itāq): Emancipation of a slave; it is irrevocable.

D) 'By Allah, there is no divorce.'

Key Takeaway
In nikah and talaq claims, the oath is on current status (not past act) to prevent deception; exception for emancipation due to irrevocability.

Competing Claims to a House: Evidence and Distribution
Ruling on distributing a disputed property with competing evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for distributing property when two claimants present evidence for different shares.

Core Idea
Evidence of whole outweighs half, yielding a 3/4 to 1/4 split.

Two people claim the same house: one says it is entirely his, the other says half is his. Both bring witnesses. How does a Hanafi judge decide? This lesson explains the unique rule for overlapping evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the possession and claims
The house is in the possession of a third party (not the claimants). One claimant claims the whole, the other claims half.
Possession is with a defendant; the claimants are plaintiffs.
2
Recognize the evidence (bayyinah)
Both claimants produce valid evidence (two male witnesses each). The judge accepts both proofs as credible.
Bayyinah establishes ownership in Hanafi law.
3
Analyze the overlap
The half claimant's evidence proves his right to half. The whole claimant's evidence proves his right to the entire house, which includes the half claimed by the other.
The whole claimant's evidence covers the contested half as well.
4
Apply the Hanafi distribution rule
The whole claimant gets 3/4 (his full claim minus the half of the half that the half claimant proved). The half claimant gets 1/4.
This is a special rule for overlapping evidence, not a general principle.
5
State the final shares
The house is divided: 3/4 to the whole claimant, 1/4 to the half claimant.
The shares sum to the whole house.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۷۷۸](۴۹) وان كانت دار فى يد رجل ادعاها اثنان احدهما جميعها و الآخر نصفها اقاما البينة فلصاحب الجميع ثلاثة ارباعها ولصاحب النصف ربعها
And if there is a house in the possession of a man, and two persons claim it: one claims all of it and the other claims half of it, and they both produce evidence, then the claimant of all gets three-fourths of it, and the claimant of half gets one-fourth of it.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage presents a specific case: a house is in someone's possession (presumably as a defendant). Two plaintiffs claim it: one says the entire house is his, the other says half is his. Both bring witnesses (bayyinah). According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the house is divided as follows: the one who claimed the whole gets three-fourths, the one who claimed half gets one-fourth. The reasoning (mentioned in the text's commentary, not in this excerpt) is that the claims overlap: the half claimant's evidence proves his right to half, but the whole claimant's evidence proves his right to the whole. Since both are credible, the whole claimant's evidence is stronger for the half that is contested, resulting in three-fourths to him and one-fourth to the half claimant. This is a unique rule in Hanafi fiqh for competing evidence on the same property.

Key Terms
البينة (al-bayyinah): Evidence, usually two male witnesses, proving a claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Half the house
B) One-fourth of the house
C) Three-fourths of the house
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
When one claimant proves full ownership and another proves half ownership with evidence, the whole claimant gets three-fourths and the half claimant gets one-fourth.

Claim of Whole vs Half a House: Division under Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on claim division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for dividing a house when one claims the whole and the other claims half, and explain the difference between the methods of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani).

Core Idea
The uncontested portion is awarded first; the contested portion is divided either equally (Abu Hanifa) or proportionally to claims (Sahibayn).

When two parties claim overlapping shares of a single property, how does a judge determine the division? This passage presents a classical intra-Hanafi dispute: one claimant says 'all of it is mine,' the other says 'half is mine.' Both produce equal evidence. The ruling differs depending on whether the property is in the hands of a third party or in the possession of the claimants themselves. The two positions of the Hanafi school—Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn—offer distinct approaches to the contested portion, revealing deeper principles about how evidence weights and uncontested portions interact in Islamic adjudication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage covers two distinct factual scenarios: first, the house is in the possession of a third party (neither claimant holds it); second, the house is in the joint possession of both claimants. Understand the legal effect of possession on burden of proof.
In the first scenario, both are plaintiffs with equal evidence; in the second, the half-claimant is a defendant regarding the half he possesses.
2
Apply Abu Hanifa's method (first scenario)
Imam Abu Hanifa separates the half that is not claimed by the half-claimant (i.e., the other half of the house) and awards it entirely to the whole claimant, since it is uncontested. The remaining half (the half claimed) is divided equally between the two because evidence is equal. This yields 3/4 for the whole claimant and 1/4 for the half-claimant.
The key principle: uncontested portion goes entirely to the one who claims it, even if it overlaps with a larger claim.
3
Apply the Sahibayn's method (first scenario)
The Sahibayn view the claims as a ratio: the whole claim is double the half claim (2:1). They divide the entire house proportionally: the whole claimant gets two-thirds, the half-claimant one-third. They do not reserve an uncontested portion but weigh the evidence according to the magnitude of the claims.
Their reasoning: evidence supporting a claim for the whole is twice as much in effect as evidence for half, so the division mirrors that ratio.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
In the first scenario, the difference is 3/4 vs 1/4 (Abu Hanifa) versus 2/3 vs 1/3 (Sahibayn). The whole claimant receives 8.33% more under Abu Hanifa. Understand that both are legitimate Hanafi positions recorded in Zahir al-Riwayah.
The text does not specify which is mufta bihi; both are relied upon in different contexts.
5
Examine the second scenario (joint possession)
When the house is in both claimants' hands, Abu Hanifa rules that the whole claimant gets the entire house. Half is from his own possession (uncontested), and the other half is from the half-claimant's possession but is awarded to the whole claimant because the half-claimant, as the possessor of that half, becomes the defendant. The whole claimant's evidence prevails, and the half-claimant can only take an oath, which does not override the evidence.
This second ruling shows how possession changes who bears the burden of proof: the possessor is the defendant; the non-possessor is the plaintiff.

Source Text

الجميع نصفها على وجه القضاء ونصفها لا على وجه القضاء
Book of Claims. The whole is three-quarters of it, and the owner of half has a quarter of it according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him. (50) And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: It is between them as a third and two-thirds. And if the house was in the hands of both, it is handed over to the owner of the whole: half of it by way of judgment and half of it not by way of judgment. (Followed by Urdu commentary explaining the rulings and the positions of the Sahibayn.)
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a house claimed by two parties: one claims the entire house, the other claims half. The matn first states the ruling of Imam Abu Hanifa (when the house is in the hands of a third party): the one claiming the whole gets three-quarters, and the one claiming half gets one-quarter. This is because the half that is not in dispute goes entirely to the claimant of the whole (since the other only claims half), and the remaining half is divided equally between them due to equal evidence, so the whole claimant gets an additional quarter, totaling three-quarters. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree: they divide the entire house in a ratio of 2:1 (three parts total), giving two-thirds to the one claiming the whole and one-third to the one claiming half. Their reasoning is that the claims are in the ratio 2:1 (whole vs half), so the evidence should be weighed accordingly. The passage then presents a second case (51) where the house is in the possession of both claimants. Here, Imam Abu Hanifa rules that the one claiming the whole gets the entire house: the half that is in his own possession is uncontested (since the other only claims half, which is precisely that half), and the other half, which is in the possession of the half-claimant, is decided in favor of the whole claimant because he is the plaintiff with evidence, and the half-claimant (being the defendant) cannot counter with evidence but only an oath, so the evidence of the whole claimant prevails. The commentator explains the principles of possession and burden of proof. The Sahibayn's position on this second case is not stated here; only Abu Hanifa's ruling is given in the matn and commentary.

Key Terms
اثلاث (athlāth): A division into three parts: two-thirds for one and one-third for the other.
بینہ (bayyinah): Legal evidence, usually two witnesses or equivalent proof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, when a house is in the possession of a third party and two claimants both bring equal evidence (one claiming the whole, the other half), what share does the whole claimant receive?
A) One-half
B) Two-thirds
C) Three-quarters
D) The entire house

Key Takeaway
Imam Abu Hanifa separates the uncontested portion before dividing the contested half equally; the Sahibayn divide the entire property proportionally to the magnitude of the claims.

Dispute Over an Animal: Using Preponderant Signs (Alāmat Rājiḥah)
Ruling on dispute resolution using birth witnesses and age correspondence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two claimants present equal evidence for an animal, identify when a preponderant sign tips the scale, and apply the rule to a new case.

Core Idea
When evidence is equal, a preponderant sign gives preference; if none, the disputed property is divided equally.

In Islamic judiciary, disputes often arise over property where both parties claim ownership with witnesses. This passage from Hanafi fiqh teaches how a judge should rule when each party brings equal proof that an animal was born in their possession. The key is to look for any external indicator, such as the animal's age matching a claimed date, which serves as a preponderant sign (alāmat rājiḥah). If no such sign exists, the animal is divided equally between them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenario
Recognize that two claimants each present valid bayyinah that the animal was born at their place and state a specific date of birth.
Both must have bayyinah; otherwise the one without evidence would be the defendant.
2
Check for a preponderant sign
Compare the animal's actual age (determined by expert examination) with the dates stated by each claimant. If the age matches one claim exactly, that is a preponderant sign (alāmat rājiḥah).
The sign is not limited to age; any reliable indicator can tip the scale.
3
Apply the ruling based on the sign
If a preponderant sign exists, award the animal to the claimant whose claim it supports. If no sign exists (age matches neither or cannot be determined), the evidence is balanced and the animal is divided equally between them.
Equal division is based on the Prophet's ruling when both brought equal witnesses.
4
Understand the supporting proofs
The commentator cites two hadiths: (a) the Prophet approved Hudhayfa's judgment based on a physical sign (camel tie), (b) the Prophet divided a camel equally when two claimants brought equal witnesses. These establish the principle.
These hadith are ahad (zanni al-thubut) but their dalaalah is qati' (clear meaning).
5
Note the broader principle
External signs (qarā'in) can break a tie when evidence is equal; otherwise, equal evidence leads to equal division. This reflects the Hanafi reliance on circumstantial indicators.
Other schools may require additional oaths or procedures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ ۲۷۸۱ (۵۲) واذا تنازعا في دابة واقام كل واحد منهما بينة انها نتجت عنده وذكرا تاريخا وسن الدابة يوافق احد
کے قبضے میں ہے۔ اس لئے یہ آدھا کل والے کومل گیا۔ اور جس آدھے پر آدھے دعویدار کا قبضہ ہے اس میں وہ مدعی علیہ ہے اور دوسرا فریق مدعی ہے اس لئے مدعی کے بینہ کو مان کر یہ آدھا بھی کل دعویدار کو دے دیا جائے گا بطور قضاء کے۔ (۲۷۸۱) (۵۲) اگر دو آدمی جھگڑے ایک جانور کے بارے میں اور ہر ایک نے قائم کیا بعینہ اس بات پر کہ اس کے یہاں پیدا ہوا ہے۔ اور دونوں نے تاریخ ذکر کی اور جانور کی عمران مین سے ایک کی موافق ہو تو وہ زیادہ بہتر ہے۔ اور اگر یہ بھی مشکل ہو جائے تو جانور دونوں کے درمیان ہوگا۔
دو آدمیوں نے ایک جانور کے بارے میں دعوی کیا کہ یہ میرا ہے اور یہ بھی کہا کہ یہ میرے یہاں پیدا ہوا ہے۔ اور دونوں نے تاریخ پیدائش بھی ذکر کی اور اس پر بینہ قائم کیا۔ مثلا ایک نے کہا سات سال پہلے پیدا ہوا ہے اور دوسرا کہتا ہے کہ دس سال پہلے پیدا ہوا ہے۔ اب جانور کی عمر دس سال ہے تو یہ دس سال والے کو دے دیا جائے گا۔
یہاں دونوں ۔ بعینہ قائم کیا ہے۔ دونوں نے تاریخ پیدائش ذکر کی ہے اس لئے کسی کو ترجیح نہیں دے سکتے البتہ جانور کی عمر جس کی تاریخ کی موافقت کرتی ہے اس کے لئے علامت راجہ ہے کہ وہ سچ کہہ رہا ہے اس لئے اس کے لئے فیصلہ ہوگا۔
حدیث میں ہے کہ حضرت حذیفہ نے اونٹ باندھنے کی علامت پر جھونپڑے کا فیصلہ فرمایا۔ حدیث یہ ہے۔عن نمران بن جارية عن ابيه ان قوما اختصموا الى النبي الله في خص كان بينهم فبعث حذيفة يقضى بينهم فقضى للذين يليهم القمط فلما رجع الى النبي هل اخبره فقال اصبت و احسنت (الف) (ابن ماجہ شریف، بابالرجلان یدعیان خص ص ۳۳۵ نمبر ۲۳۴۳) اس
حدیث میں علامت راجہ پر چھونپڑے کا فیصلہ فرمایا۔ اسی طرح جانور کی عمر علامت راجہ ہے وہ جس کی موافقت کرے گی جانور اسی کا ہوگا۔ اور اگر جانور کی عمر کسی کی تاریخ کی موافقت نہیں کرتی ہو مثلا جانور کی عمر آٹھ سال کی ہو نہ سات نہ دس سال کی تو یہ علامت راجہ نہیں ہے۔ اور دونوں نے بینہ پیش کیا ہے اور دونوں اپنے یہاں پیدا ہونے کے گواہ لائے ہیں اس لئے دونوں کے لئے آدھے آدھے جانور کا فیصلہ ہوگا (۲) حدیث گزر چکی ہے۔ عن ابی موسی اشعری ان رجلين ادعيا بعيرا على عهد النبي الله فبعث كل واحد منهما شاهدين فقسمه النبي الا الله بينهما نصفين (ب) (ابوداؤد شریف، باب الرجلین یدعیان شیئا ولیس بینھما بی ص ۱۵۳ نمبر ۳۶۱۵) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ دونوں گواہ برابر تھے تو دونوں کو آدھا آدھا دیا گیا (۳) اثر میں ہے۔ عن عبد الرحمن بن ابی لیلی قال اختصم رجلان الى ابي الدرداء فى فرس فاقام كل واحد منهما البينة انه انتج عنده لم يبعه ولم يهبه وجاء الآخر بمثل ذلك فقال حاشیہ : (الف) ایک قوم حضور کے پاس جھونپڑے کے بارے میں جھگڑا لیکر آئی ۔ آپ نے اس کے بارے میں حضرت حذیفہ کو فیصلے کے لئے بھیجا۔ انہوں نے اس کے لئے فیصلہ کیا جس کی اونٹ کی قطار اس کے متصل تھی۔ پس جب واپس آئے اور حضور گو اس کی خبر دی تو آپ نے فرمایا ٹھیک کیا، اچھا کیا (ب) حضور کے زمانے میں دو آدمیوں نے اونٹ کا دعوی کیا اور ہر ایک نے دو دو گواہیاں پیش کیں تو آپ نے دونوں کے درمیان آدھا آدھا تقسیم فرمایا۔
(52) And if two persons dispute concerning an animal, and each of them produces evidence that it was born at his place, and they mention a date, and the age of the animal agrees with one of them... [Urdu commentary explaining that if the animal's age matches one of their stated dates, it is given to that person; if not, the animal is divided between them.] Hadith: From Nimran ibn Jariya from his father that some people disputed before the Prophet about a hut, so he sent Hudhayfa to judge between them, and he judged in favor of those whose camel tie was adjacent. When he returned and informed the Prophet, he said: 'You have done correctly and well.' (Ibn Majah). Another hadith: From Abu Musa al-Ash'ari that two men claimed a camel during the time of the Prophet, and each brought two witnesses, so the Prophet divided it equally between them. (Abu Dawud). Also an athar from Abd al-Rahman ibn Abi Layla: two men disputed before Abu al-Darda about a horse, each brought evidence that it was born at his place and he had not sold or given it away, and the other brought similar evidence, then he said...
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling for a dispute over an animal where both claimants present evidence (bayyinah) that the animal was born in their possession, and they state specific dates. The commentator explains that the animal's age is used as a 'rajih' (preponderant) indicator: if the animal's actual age matches the date stated by one of them, that person is deemed truthful and awarded the animal. If the age does not match either date, the evidence is considered equal, and the animal is divided equally. The commentator supports this with three sources: (1) A hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) approved Hudhayfa's judgment based on a physical sign (the camel tie adjacent to a hut) as a preponderant indicator; (2) A hadith where the Prophet divided a camel equally when two claimants brought equal witnesses; (3) An athar (report from a Companion) of Abu al-Darda dealing with a horse dispute. The principle is that when evidence is equal, the judge looks for any additional indication (qarīna) to prefer one side; if none exists, the object is divided. This reflects the Hanafi reliance on external signs (amārāt) to resolve conflicts when direct evidence is balanced.

Key Terms
علامت راجہ (alāmat rājiḥah): A preponderant sign or indicator that helps resolve a conflict when evidence is equal, such as the animal's age corresponding to a claimed date.
بینہ (bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically two male witnesses or one male and two females, but can also include strong circumstantial evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dispute over an animal where both claimants present evidence that it was born at their place with specific dates, what is the ruling if the animal's age matches the date given by one of them?
A) The animal is divided equally
B) The animal is given to the one whose date matches
C) Both are asked to take an oath
D) The judge declares the animal lost

Key Takeaway
When evidence is equal, a preponderant sign (alāmat rājiḥah) determines ownership; if none, the disputed property is divided equally.

Dispute over Animal: Birth Claim with Date Matching
Ruling on animal dispute with birth evidence and date matching

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for resolving a dispute over an animal when both claimants provide birth evidence, and apply the concept of preponderant sign (alama rajiha) to determine entitlement.

Core Idea
Age match acts as a preponderant sign (alama rajiha) to decide animal ownership when both claimants present equal birth evidence.

When two parties each claim an animal was born in their possession, and both bring witnesses, the court must decide. How does a qadi (judge) break the tie? The Hanafi school uses external signs (alama rajiha), including matching the animal's age to one claimant's date. This lesson explores that rule and its proof from hadith and athar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute and the initial evidence
Two claimants each present a bayyina (witnesses) that the animal was born in their possession. Both mention a specific date of birth. The animal's age is also known (by inspection).
The bayyina establishes a prima facie case for each claimant.
2
Apply the preponderant sign (alama rajiha)
If the animal's age matches one claimant's date exactly, that match serves as a preponderant sign. The claimant with the matching date is deemed more entitled (awla) to the animal.
The sign is external and objective, not based on witness credibility.
3
Handle the ambiguous case
If the age does not match either date (or matches both equally), the evidence is balanced. In that case, the animal is divided equally between the claimants (nisfayn).
This follows the hadith of the Prophet dividing a camel equally when both brought witnesses.
4
Examine the proof from the first hadith (tether proximity)
The hadith of Hudhayfa shows that a preponderant sign (proximity of camel tethers) can decide a dispute. The Prophet approved this judgment, establishing the principle that external signs are valid evidence.
The 'qamat' (tether) indicates possession and care.
5
Examine the proof from the second hadith and the athar
The hadith of Abu Musa al-Ash'ari shows that when evidence is equal, the object is divided equally. The athar of Abu al-Darda applies the same rule to a horse. These support the ruling for the ambiguous case.
Thus the ruling has a strong basis in practice of the Prophet and the Companions.

Source Text

Book of Claims. (2781) (52) If two litigants dispute over an animal, and each one establishes evidence that it was born in his possession, and they mention a date, and the age of the animal matches one of the dates, then that one is more entitled. And if that is ambiguous, then it is between them. ... Hadith from Nimran ibn Jariya from his father that a people disputed before the Prophet about a hut, and he sent Hudhayfa to judge between them, and he judged for those whose camel tether was adjacent. When he returned and told the Prophet, he said: 'You were correct and did well.' (Ibn Majah, ...). Hadith from Abu Musa al-Ash'ari that two men claimed a camel in the time of the Prophet, and each brought two witnesses, and the Prophet divided it between them in halves. (Abu Dawud, ...). Athar from Abd al-Rahman ibn Abi Layla: Two men disputed before Abu al-Darda about a horse; each established evidence that it was born in his possession, he did not sell it nor give it as a gift, and the other came with the same. Abu al-Darda said: 'One of you is lying,' and divided it between them in halves. (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, ...).
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Book of Claims. The author presents a case where two people each claim an animal was born in their possession, and both provide evidence with specific dates. If the animal's physical age matches one claimant's date, that match serves as a preponderant sign (alama rajiha), giving that claimant priority. If the age does not match either date, the animal is divided equally between them. The commentary explains the reasoning using the concept of preponderant signs, supported by three narrations: the hadith of Hudhayfa judging a hut based on proximity of camel tethers, the hadith of Abu Musa where the Prophet divided a camel when both brought equal witnesses, and the athar of Abu al-Darda dividing a horse equally when evidence was equal. These narrations establish the principle that when external signs or evidence are equal, the object is shared.

Key Terms
علامت راجحہ (alama rajiha): A preponderant sign that tips the scale in favor of one claimant when evidence is otherwise balanced.
بینہ (bayyina): Evidence, usually two witnesses, establishing a claim in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The animal is divided equally between them.
B) The animal is given to the claimant whose date matches the age.
C) The animal remains with the current possessor.
D) The judge selects randomly.

Key Takeaway
When two claimants present equal birth evidence for an animal, the age match serves as a preponderant sign giving entitlement; if no match, the animal is divided.

Preponderant Sign in Disputes over Movable Property
Rulings on disputes where physical control indicates ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of alama rajiha (preponderant sign) to determine physical control as an indicator of ownership in disputes.

Core Idea
Physical control (riding, wearing, owning the load) is a preponderant sign of ownership in disputes.

In disputes over movable property when neither party has clear evidence, Islamic judges rely on external signs that indicate ownership. This lesson presents two classical Hanafi rulings where physical connection to an animal or garment decides the case. The principle is based on the hadith of Hudhayfa, which allows judgment by preponderant signs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what is being judged and the outcome: in each case, one party is declared 'awla' (more entitled) based on physical control.
The ruling is not about ownership per se but about who is preferred in the dispute.
2
Locate the dalil
The text references a previous hadith (Hudhayfa) as the proof. Identify that the dalil is a Sunnah ahad (single narration) that establishes the validity of using preponderant signs.
The hadith is not quoted verbatim; it is assumed known.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the hadith supports the rulings: the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم used a preponderant sign (physical control) to decide a dispute, so the same principle applies here.
The connection is by qiyas: the rider's control is analogous to the sign in the hadith.
4
Note the condition
The principle applies only when there is no other evidence (بشرطیکہ کوئی اور دلیل نہ ہو). Identify that this is a condition (shart) for using the preponderant sign.
If evidence exists, it takes precedence over the sign.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school considers these rulings as valid judicial determinations (sahih) and part of Zahir al-Riwayah. Note that other schools may require additional evidence, but the Hanafi view uses the sign as a decisive factor.
Cross-madhhab: some schools may require oath-taking or witnesses even with physical control.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۷۸۲] (۵۳) واذا تنازعا على دابة احدهما راكبها والآخر متعلق بلجامها فالراكب اولی. [۲۷۸۳] (۵۴) و كذلك اذا تنازعا بعيرا و عليه حمل لاحدهما وصاحب الحمل اولی و کذلک اذا تنازعا قميصا احدهما لابسه والآخر متعلق بكمه فصاحب القميص اولی. علامت راجہ جس کی ہو چیز اس کی ہوگی بشر طیکہ کوئی اور دلیل نہ ہو۔ حدیث حذیفہ گزرگئی (ابن ماجہ شریف نمبر ۲۳۴۳).
[2782] (53) If two dispute over an animal, one riding it and the other holding its bridle, then the rider is more entitled. [2783] (54) Similarly, if two dispute over a camel, and upon it is a load for one of them, then the owner of the load is more entitled; and likewise if two dispute over a shirt, one wearing it and the other holding its sleeve, then the wearer of the shirt is more entitled. Whichever has the preponderant sign, the item is for him, provided there is no other evidence. The hadith of Hudhayfa has passed (Ibn Majah no. 2343).
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage contains two rulings from the Book of Claims, each based on the principle of preponderant signs (alama rajiha). The first ruling (53) states that if two people dispute over an animal, the one riding it is preferred over the one merely holding the bridle. The rider has greater physical control, which is a sign of ownership. The second ruling (54) gives two similar examples: if a camel has a load belonging to one of the disputants, the owner of the load is preferred; if two dispute over a shirt, the wearer is preferred over the one holding the sleeve. The commentary explains that each case illustrates the same principle: the person with the stronger external connection to the item is more likely the owner, absent any contrary evidence. This principle is supported by the hadith of Hudhayfa (previously cited) where judgment was based on a preponderant sign.

Key Terms
علامت راجحہ (alama rajiha): A preponderant sign that indicates ownership through physical control or connection.
اولی (awla): More entitled or preferred in a legal dispute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling, if two people dispute over a camel and one has a load on it, who is preferred?
A) The person holding the bridle.
B) The owner of the load.
C) Anyone who brings witnesses.
D) The camel is divided.

Key Takeaway
In disputes over movable property lacking evidence, the Hanafi school uses physical control as a preponderant sign to determine who is more entitled.

Preponderant Signs in Property Disputes
Hanafi rules on deciding property disputes using physical possession indicators and equal evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for when a preponderant sign decides ownership and apply it to cases involving physical possession indicators.

Core Idea
Physical possession like riding or wearing a garment is a preponderant sign that awards ownership.

This lesson from Al-Sharh al-Samari explains how Hanafi fiqh resolves disputes over movable property when one party has a visible indicator of possession. The key principle is that such an 'alamah rajihah' (preponderant sign) serves as evidence, tipping the scale in favor of the party with the indicator. The passage also addresses the rare case where both parties present equal evidence without any physical sign, leading to equal division.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed object and parties' claims
Determine the object (animal, garment) and what each party claims. Note any physical contact or possession.
2
Check for a preponderant sign (alamah rajihah)
See if one party has a distinctive physical indicator: riding, having a load on it, wearing it. This elevates their claim above mere holding.
Holding the rein or sleeve is not a preponderant sign.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule to the scenario
If a preponderant sign exists and no counter-evidence is provided, the party with the sign is awarded the object. If both have equal evidence and no sign, the object is divided equally.
The rule is based on the hadith of Hudhayfa and the companion Abu al-Darda's practice.
4
Note the evidence (dalil)
The hadith from Ibn Majah (no. 2343) and the action of Abu al-Darda show that physical indicators are accepted as evidence in Hanafi fiqh.
Other schools may not give such weight to physical possession alone.
5
Differentiate from equal evidence case
When both parties present formal witnesses and no preponderant sign exists, the judge declares one of them false and divides equally, as Abu al-Darda did with the horse.
This is a specific exception; the general rule is to award to the sign holder.

و عليه حمل لاحدهما وصاحب الحمل اولی و کذلک اذا تنازعا قميصا احدهما ابو الدرداء ان احدكما كاذب فقسمه بينهما نصفين (الف) (سن للمبتی ، باب المند اعسين بيتد اعیان مالم يكن في يد واحد منهما و یقیم کل واحد منهمابیته بدعواه ج عاشرص ۴۳۹ نمبر ۲۱۲۴۲) اس اثر میں تو دونوں نے اپنے یہاں پیدا ہونے کا بھی دعوی کیا اور اس پر بینہ پیش کیا اور حضرت ابو درداء نے دونوں کے درمیان آدھے آدھے کا فیصلہ فرمایا۔ لفت نتجبت : پیدا ہوا۔ [۲۷۸۲] (۵۳) اگر دو آدمیوں نے جھگڑا کیا ایک جانور کے بارے میں۔ ان میں سے ایک اس پر سوار ہے اور دوسرا ان کی لگام پکڑے ہوئے ہے تو سوار اولی ہے۔ جو سوار ہے اس کا قبضہ صرف لگام پکڑنے والے سے زیادہ ہے۔ اس لئے علامت راجہ یہی ہے کہ جانور سوار والے کا ہے اس لئے اس کے حق میں فیصلہ ہوگا۔ سیاس وقت ہے جبکہ کوئی علامت نہ ہو۔ یہ کئی مسائل اس اصول پر ہیں کہ جن کے پاس علامت راجہ ہو چیز اس کی قرار دی جائے گی۔ اور علامت راجحہ کی دلیل ابن ماجہ کی حدیث حذیفہ گزرگئی (ابن ماجہ شریف نمبر ۲۳۴۳) ۲۷۸۳] (۵۴) ایسے ہی اگر جھگڑا کرے دو آدمی اونٹ میں ۔ اور اس پر ایک آدمی کا بوجھ ہو تو بوجھ والا اولی ہے۔ اور ایسے ہی جھگڑا کرے دو آدمی قمیص میں۔ ان میں سے ایک اس کو پہنے ہوئے ہو اور دوسرا آستین پکڑے ہوئے ہو تو سہنے والا اولی ہے۔ جس کا بوجھ اونٹ پر ہے ظاہر ہے کہ اس کا بوجھ ہونا اس بات کی علامت راجہ ہے کہ اونٹ اسی کا ہے تب ہی تو اس نے اس پر بوجھ لا دا ہے اور دوسرا نہ لا دسکا۔ اس لئے اونٹ بوجھ والے کا ہوگا۔ اسی طرح جو آدمی قمیص پہنے ہوئے ہے علامت راجہ یہی ہے کہ قمیص اسی کی ہے۔ کیونکہ پہنا اس بات کی علامت ہے کہ قمیص اس کی ہے ورنہ وہ کیسے پہن لیتا۔ اور دوسرے کی نہیں ہے اس لئے وہ صرف آستین پکڑے ہوئے ہے۔ اصول علامت راجہ جس کی ہو چیز اس کی ہوگی بشر طیکہ کوئی اور دلیل نہ ہو۔ حمل : بوجھ۔ کم : آستین۔ حاشیه : (الف) دو آدمی حضرت ابو درداء کے پاس گھوڑے کے بارے میں جھگڑا لے کر آئے۔ پھر ہر ایک نے مبینہ قائم کیا کہ اس کے پاس پیدا ہوا ہے۔ نہ اس کو بیچا ہے نہ اس کو ہبہ کیا ہے۔ اور دوسرا بھی اسی طرح گواہ لے کر آیا تو حضرت ابود، دانہ نے فرمایا تم دونوں میں سے ایک جھوٹا ہے۔ اور دونوں کے درمیان آدھا آدھا تقسیم کر دیا۔ لابسه والآخر متعلق بكمه فاللابس اولی
The Samari Commentary Volume Four) Book of Claims. If they dispute over a riding animal, one riding it and the other holding its rein, the rider is more entitled. Similarly, if they dispute over a camel with a load belonging to one of them, the owner of the load is more entitled. Likewise, if they dispute over a shirt, one of them [wearing it]... Abu al-Darda said one of you is lying, so he divided it equally between them. (Reference) In this narration, both claimed that it was born in their possession and presented evidence, and Abu al-Darda decided by half and half. [2782] (53) If two people dispute over an animal, one riding it and the other holding its rein, the rider is more entitled. Because the rider has stronger possession than the one holding the rein. Therefore the preponderant sign is that the animal belongs to the rider, so judgment is given in his favor. This is when there is no other sign. These several cases are based on the principle that whoever has a preponderant sign, the thing is awarded to him. The evidence for the preponderant sign is the hadith of Hudhayfa from Ibn Majah (no. 2343). [2783] (54) Similarly, if two dispute over a camel and on it is a load belonging to one, the owner of the load is more entitled. Similarly, if two dispute over a shirt, one wearing it and the other holding its sleeve, the wearer is more entitled. The one whose load is on the camel: obviously his load being a preponderant sign that the camel is his, because he placed his load on it and the other could not. So the camel belongs to the load owner. Similarly, the person wearing the shirt: the preponderant sign is that the shirt is his, because wearing is a sign of ownership; otherwise how could he wear it? The other only holds the sleeve. Principle: Whoever has a preponderant sign, the thing is his, provided there is no other evidence. Load = burden, sleeve = sleeve. Footnote: Two people came to Abu al-Darda disputing over a horse. Each established evidence that it was born in his possession, not sold nor gifted. The other also brought witnesses. Abu al-Darda said: one of you is lying, and divided it equally between them. And (on page 124) the wearer and the other holding its sleeve: the wearer is more entitled.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage from the Hanafi commentary Al Sharh Ul Sameri discusses rules for deciding property disputes in the Book of Claims (Kitab al-Da'wa). It presents two categories: cases where one party has a physical indicator of possession (alamah rajihah), and a case where both have equal evidence. The rulings are based on a hadith from Ibn Majah (no. 2343) and the practice of the Companion Abu al-Darda. The first three examples establish that when one party physically controls the object in a distinctive way (riding, having a load on a camel, wearing a garment), that party is preferred because their possession is stronger and serves as a preponderant indication of ownership. The principle is that the person with the preponderant sign gets the thing, as long as no other evidence contradicts. The second example is the case of two claimants who each present evidence of ownership without any physical indicator. Here, Abu al-Darda declared one of them a liar and divided the property equally, because neither had superior possession. This illustrates that when both have equal evidence and no preponderant sign, the judge may divide the disputed property. The commentary emphasizes that these rules apply only when there is no stronger counter-evidence.

Key Terms
علامت راجحہ (alamah rajihah): Preponderant sign or indicator of ownership, such as riding an animal, having a load on it, or wearing a garment.
مدعی (mudda'i): The claimant who makes an assertion; the one who must provide evidence.
مدعی علیہ (mudda'a 'alayh): The defendant against whom the claim is made; he must take an oath if the claimant lacks evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi rule on preponderant signs, in a dispute over a horse where one person is riding and the other is holding the rein, who is awarded the horse?
A) The rider
B) The rein holder
C) Both equally
D) Neither until evidence is brought

Key Takeaway
The preponderant sign of physical possession (like riding or wearing) decides ownership in Hanafi property disputes unless counter-evidence exists.

Ruling on Refusal of Oath (Nukul) in Disputes
Explains the ruling and proof for nukul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on nukul (refusal of oath) and explain its legal effect using the hadith proof.

Core Idea
Nukul (refusal to take an oath) is treated as an admission or a second witness, making the opponent's claim binding.

In disputes where both parties are claimants and defendants, the judge may require them to take oaths. This lesson explains what happens when one party refuses. The Hanafi school holds that such refusal (nukul) establishes the opponent's claim, based on a prophetic hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenario
Note that the passage deals with a mutual claim where both parties are both claimants and defendants (e.g., a seller and buyer disputing ownership). The judge requires both to swear.
2
Understand the ruling
If one party refuses the oath (nukul), the claim of the other party becomes binding upon the refuser. The judge decides in favor of the opponent.
This is the central ruling.
3
Analyze the two possible intentions
The commentary explains two possible meanings behind the refusal: either tacit admission that the opponent's claim is correct, or a choice to forfeit the disputed item rather than swear by Allah. In both cases, the judge rules against the refuser.
The legal effect is independent of the refuser's actual intention.
4
Examine the hadith proof
The hadith from Ibn Majah (and al-Dar Qutni) states that a husband's refusal to swear in a divorce claim is equivalent to a second witness, making the divorce valid. This establishes the principle that nukul functions as proof against the refuser.
The hadith is ahad in transmission (zanni al-thubut) but its meaning is clear (qati al-dalalah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فان نكل احدهما عن اليمين لزمه دعوى الآخر [۲۷۸۸] (۵۹) پس اگر دونوں میں سے ایک قسم سے انکار کرے تو اس پر دوسرے کا دعوی لازم ہوگا۔
بائع اور مشتری کو قسم کھانے کے لئے کہا۔ پس دونوں میں سے ایک نے قسم کھانے سے انکار کر دیا تو اس کے انکار کے دو مطالب ہیں۔ ایک تو یہ کہ میں دبے زبان سے اقرار کرتا ہوں کہ خصم کا دعوی صحیح ہے۔ اور دوسرا مطلب یہ ہے کہ خصم کا دعوی صحیح تو نہیں ہے لیکن چلو اپنی چیز دے دیتا ہوں یہ اللہ کے عظیم نام کے ساتھ قسم کھانے سے بہتر ہے۔ تاہم دونوں صورتوں میں قاضی مد مقابل کے دعوی کے مطابق فیصلہ
کر دے گا۔
حدیث میں اس کا ثبوت ہے۔ عن عمر بن شعیب عن ابيه عن جده عن النبي الله قال اذا ادعت المرأة طلاق زوجها فجائت على ذلك بشاهد عدل استحلف زوجها فان حلف بطلت شهادة الشاهد وان نكل فنكوله بمنزلة شاهد آخر وجاز طلاقه (الف) (ابن ماجہ شریف، باب الرجل بجد الطلاق ص ۲۹۲ نمبر ۲۰۳۸ ردار قطنی ، کتاب الوکاله ج رابع ص ۶؟ نمبر ۴۲۹۵) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ قسم سے انکار کرنا دوسرے گواہ کے درجے میں ہے۔ اور اس سے مدعی کے دعوی کے مطابق فیصلہ کر دیا
جائے گا۔
If one of them refuses the oath, the claim of the other becomes binding upon him. [2788] (59) So if one of the two refuses the oath, the claim of the other becomes incumbent upon him. The seller and buyer were asked to take an oath. Then if one of them refuses the oath, his refusal has two meanings: one is that he tacitly admits that the opponent's claim is correct; the other is that the opponent's claim is not correct, but he says, 'I give up my property, it is better than taking an oath by the great name of Allah.' In both cases, the judge will decide according to the opponent's claim. There is proof of this in the hadith: 'From 'Amr ibn Shu'ayb, from his father, from his grandfather, from the Prophet ﷺ, he said: If a woman claims divorce from her husband and brings a single just witness, the husband is made to take an oath. If he swears, the witness's testimony is invalidated. If he refuses (nukul), his refusal is in the position of another witness, and her divorce becomes permissible.' This hadith indicates that refusal of the oath is equivalent to a second witness, and thus the judge decides according to the claimant's claim.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It states the ruling on nukul (refusal to take an oath) when two parties are both claimants and defendants in a dispute and are required to take oaths. The author explains that if one refuses, the other's claim becomes binding. The commentary elaborates that the refusal is interpreted either as an admission of the opponent's claim or as a preference to forfeit the object of dispute rather than swear. In both cases, the judge rules against the refuser. The hadith from Ibn Majah is cited as proof: the refusal of the husband to take an oath in a divorce claim is treated as a second witness, making the divorce valid. This establishes that nukul has the legal effect of establishing the opponent's claim.

Key Terms
نكل (nukul): Refusal to take an oath after being required by the judge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited, what is the legal effect of a husband's nukul (refusal to take an oath) in a divorce claim?
A) The divorce is invalidated
B) The nukul is equivalent to a second witness, making the divorce valid
C) The husband must be imprisoned
D) The case is dismissed

Key Takeaway
Nukul (refusal of oath) in Hanafi fiqh is equivalent to a second witness, making the opponent's claim binding on the refuser.

Disputes on Accessories: No Mutual Oaths
Explains no mutual oaths for accessory disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a dispute in a sale contract does not require mutual oaths (tahaluf) and state which party takes the oath.

Core Idea
When dispute is not in the core contract, only the defendant takes an oath.

In sale disputes, mutual oaths are needed when both parties are simultaneously claimants and defendants, such as when they disagree on price or subject matter. However, when the disagreement concerns an accessory like the payment term, an option, or whether part of the price was paid, only one party is the claimant and the other is the defendant. This lesson clarifies that in such cases, only the defendant takes an oath, following the general principle from the hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core of the contract
The essential elements of a sale are the price and the subject matter. If the dispute is about these, mutual oaths are needed. If the dispute is about something else (like term, option, or partial payment), it is an accessory.
2
Classify the dispute as accessory
Determine if the disagreement is about a term for payment, a condition of option, or receipt of part of the price. These are not part of the essence of the contract.
Examples from the text: term (ajal), option (khiyar al-shart), receipt of part price.
3
Determine who is claimant and who is defendant
The party asserting the accessory (e.g., claiming a longer term, an option, or that part price was paid) is the claimant. The party denying it is the defendant.
Claimant bears the burden of proof (must produce witness).
4
Apply the hadith: the oath is on the defendant
The Prophet (ﷺ) judged that the oath is upon the defendant. If the claimant has no witness, the defendant's statement with his oath is accepted.
Hadith: قضى باليمين على المدعى عليه
5
Conclude that mutual oaths are not required
Since only one party is the defendant, tahaluf (both taking oaths) is unnecessary. The defendant alone swears.
Mutual oaths only apply when both are claimants and defendants simultaneously.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۷۸۹] (۲۰) اور اگر دونوں نے اختلاف کیا مدت میں یا خیار شرط میں یا بعض قیمت وصول کرنے میں تو دونوں سے قسم نہیں لے جائے گی ، بات اس کی مانی جائے گی جو خیار شرط اور مدت کا انکار کرتا ہو تم کے ساتھ۔
بائع اور مشتری نے مدت کے بارے میں اختلاف کیا۔ مثلا بائع کہتا ہے کہ ایک ہفتے میں قیمت دینا طے پائی ہے اور مشتری کہتا ہے کہ دو ہفتے میں دینے کا وعدہ ہے۔ یہاں قیمت کی کمی زیادتی میں اختلاف نہیں ہے اس پر دونوں متفق ہیں البتہ قیمت کب ادا کریں گے اس میں اختلاف ہے۔ تو یہ اختلاف اصل عقد میں نہیں ہوا بلکہ دور کی صفت میں اختلاف ہوا کیونکہ نقد قیمت دے تو مدت متعین کرنے کی کوئی ضرورت نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے اس میں دونوں مدعی اور دونوں مدعی علیہ نہیں ہوں گے بلکہ جس نے مدت لی ہے صرف وہ مدعی ہے اور مد مقابل مدعی علیہ ہے اور وہی منکر ہے۔ اور مدعی کے پاس گواہ نہیں ہے تو دونوں کو قسم نہیں کھلا ئیں گے بلکہ صرف منکر کی بات قسم کے ساتھ مان لی جائے گی۔ اسی طرح خیار شرط اصل عقد میں سے نہیں ہے کیونکہ بغیر خیار شرط کے ہی بیچ منعقد ہوتی ہے۔ اور یہی حال ہے بعض ثمن کے وصول کرنے میں اختلاف کا کہ اصل عقد میں اختلاف نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ پوری قیمت دینا ہی اصل ہے۔ اس لئے خیار شرط جس نے لی ہے وہ مدعی ہے اور دوسرا مدعی علیہ اور منکر ہے۔ اس لئے دونوں پر قسم نہیں ہوگی بلکہ مدعی کے پاس گواہ نہ ہو تو منکر کی بات قسم کے ساتھ مانی جائے گی۔ آدھے من پر قبضہ کرنے کا جو دعوی کرتا ہے وہ مردگی ہے اور جو اس کا انکار کرتا ہے وہ مدعی علیہ اور منکر ہے ۔ اس لئے مدعی کے پاس گواہ نہیں ہے تو منکر کی بات قسم کے ساتھ مانی جائے گی۔
اصول یہ مسائل اس اصول پر ہیں کہ دونوں مدعی اور دونوں مدعی علیہ نہ بن سکتے ہوں بلکہ ایک مدعی اور دوسرا مدعی علیہ ہو تو دونوں پر ختم نہیں ہوگی۔ بلکہ مدعی کے پاس گواہ نہ ہو تو مدعی علیہ کی بات قسم کے ساتھ مانی جائے گی۔ حدیث گزر چکی ہے۔ کتب الی ابن عباس ان رسول الله قضى باليمين على المدعى عليه (الف) (ابوداؤد شریف، باب الیمین علی المدعی علیہ ص ۱۵۳ نمبر ۱۳۶۱۹ / ترمذی شریف، باب ما جاء فی ان البیہ علی المدعی والیمین علی المدعی علیہ ص ۲۴۹ نمبر ۱۳۴۲ ) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ مدعی علیہ پر قسم کا فیصلہ کیا گیا ہے۔
ان الاجل : مدت استیفاء : وفی سے مشتق ہے وصول کرنا۔ تحالف : باب مفاعلہ سے ہے دونوں طرف سے قسم لینا۔
[2789] (60) And if they disagree regarding the term, or regarding the condition of option, or regarding the receipt of part of the price, then they will not be required to take oaths [from both] but the statement of the one who denies the option and the term will be accepted with his oath. 
The seller and buyer disagreed about the term. For example, the seller says it was agreed to pay the price within one week, and the buyer says the promise was to pay within two weeks. Here there is no disagreement about the price itself, they both agree on it; rather, the disagreement is about when the price will be paid. This disagreement is not in the essence of the contract but in a description of the payment term, because if the price is cash, there is no need to specify a term. Therefore, in this case, they are not both claimants and defendants; rather, the one who claims the term is the claimant, and the other is the defendant and denier. Since the claimant has no witness, they will not be required to take oaths, but the statement of the denier will be accepted with his oath. Similarly, the condition of option is not part of the essence of the contract because the sale is valid without it. The same applies to disagreement about receipt of part of the price: there is no dispute in the essence of the contract because paying the full price is the norm. Thus, the one who claims the option is the claimant, and the other is the defendant and denier. Therefore, no mutual oaths will be taken; if the claimant has no witness, the statement of the denier will be accepted with his oath. The one who claims to have taken possession of half a man of goods is the claimant, and the one who denies it is the defendant and denier. So if the claimant has no witness, the statement of the denier is accepted with his oath. 
The principle underlying these issues is that when they cannot both be claimants and defendants, but one is claimant and the other defendant, then there will be no tahaluf (mutual oaths); rather, if the claimant has no witness, the statement of the defendant is accepted with his oath. The hadith has already been mentioned: He wrote to Ibn Abbas that the Messenger of Allah ﷺ judged that the oath is upon the defendant. This hadith states that the oath is decided upon the defendant. 
Al-ajal: meaning term; al-istifa: derived from wafa, meaning receipt; tahaluf: from the form mufa'ala, meaning the taking of oaths from both sides.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It addresses cases where the dispute between buyer and seller is not about the essence of the contract (e.g., price or subject matter) but about an accessory like the payment term, an option to rescind, or whether part of the price has been paid. The author rules that in such cases, the parties are not both claimants and defendants simultaneously; only one is the claimant (the one asserting the accessory) and the other is the defendant (denying it). The general rule is that the oath is upon the defendant, as per the hadith. Therefore, mutual oaths (tahaluf) are not required; instead, the defendant's statement is accepted with his oath if the claimant has no witness. The commentary provides examples: if they disagree on the term, the buyer (claiming longer term) is the claimant, the seller is defendant; if they disagree on an option, the one claiming it is claimant; if they disagree on receipt of part price, the one claiming receipt is claimant. In each case, only the defendant takes an oath. The principle is that when the dispute is not in the core contract, they cannot both be considered claimants, so the default rule applies: the defendant's oath is decisive.

Key Terms
اجل (ajal): The term for payment of the price
خیار شرط (khiyar al-shart): A condition stipulating an option to confirm or rescind the sale within a specified period
تحالف (tahaluj): Mutual oaths taken by both parties in a dispute

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the buyer and seller disagree about whether the price was to be paid in one week or two weeks, who is considered the defendant?
A) The buyer, because he claims a longer term
B) The seller, because he denies the longer term
C) Both are defendants
D) Neither is defendant

Key Takeaway
In disputes over accessories to a sale contract, only the defendant (the denier) takes an oath, not both parties.
