Prohibition of Seeking Judicial Authority (Qada)
Ruling with proof on seeking judicial authority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on seeking the position of qadi and identify the hadith proofs that support it.

Core Idea
Seeking the office of qadi is discouraged unless necessity demands, due to immense responsibility and the Prophet's warnings.

This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on a critical issue: whether it is permissible to seek or ask for the position of a judge (qadi). The author cites several hadith from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that strongly discourage such ambition. By examining these proofs, you will understand why the Hanafi school considers seeking judicial authority as makruh tanzih (discouraged) and under what circumstances it may become permissible or even necessary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the act under discussion is seeking the position of qadi (or any judicial authority). The ruling is stated at the beginning: 'ولا ينبغي ان يطلب الولاية ولايسألها' meaning it is not appropriate to seek or ask for it.
The ruling here is makruh tanzih, not haram, as later scholars clarify conditions.
2
Locate the dalil (proofs)
Identify the three hadith cited: (1) the hadith of Abu Dharr prohibiting leadership over even two persons, (2) the hadith of Abd al-Rahman ibn Samurah forbidding asking for authority, and (3) the hadith of Abu Musa where the Prophet refused to appoint those who asked or were eager.
All hadith are from Bukhari and Muslim, making them authentic but ahad (single narration).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (how each hadith proves the ruling)
For the first hadith: the Prophet's command 'لا تامرن على اثنين' is a prohibition (nahy), indicating avoidance is obligatory. For the second: 'لا تسأل الامارة' directly forbids asking. For the third: the Prophet's action shows refusal to appoint those who ask. Together, they prove that seeking is blameworthy.
The imperative form in Arabic generally indicates prohibition, giving the ruling its weight.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (scholarly difference)
While the Hanafi position is that seeking is makruh tanzih, some Shafi'i scholars allow it if the person is qualified and the community needs him. The passage does not elaborate, but the Hanafi school maintains that even in that case, seeking is not ideal.
This difference stems from varying interpretations of the hadith and the concept of necessity.
5
Evaluate the tarjih (preferred view)
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) in the Hanafi school is that seeking qada is not recommended unless one is confident of ability and there is no other qualified person. The ruling is based on the hadith and the principle of avoiding fitna and sin.
This view is upheld by later Hanafi authorities and is the standard for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب آداب القاضي
[2891](٤) ولا ينبغي ان يطلب الولاية ولايسألها :  حديث میں ہے کہ قاضی بنایا گیا تو سمجھو کہ بغیر چھری کے ذبح کیا گیا۔ جس سے معلوم ہو کہ عاجز ہو تو قضاء لینا اچھا نہیں ہے (٢) دوسری حدیث میں اس کی تصریح ہے۔ عن ابی ذر ان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قال يا ابا ذر انی اراک ضعیفا وانی احب لك ما احب لنفسي لا تامرن على اثنين ولا تولين مال يتيم (مسلم شریف، باب کرابتہ الامارة بغير ضرورة، ج ٢ ص ۱۲۱، نمبر ۱۸۲۶، ابو داؤد شریف، باب ما جاء فی الدخول فی الوصایا، ص ٤٠، نمبر ۲۸۶۸) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ آدمی قضاء سے عاجز ہو تو قضاء نہ لے(٣) اوپر کی آیت میں بھی ہے کہ اگر خواہش نفس کی اتباع کی تو گمراہ ہو جائے گا۔ اس لئے اگر عاجزی کا خوف ہو تو قضاء نہ لے تاکہ گمراہ نہ ہو۔  (٤) نہ ولایت کی درخواست کرنا مناسب ہے اور نہ اس کا مانگتا۔ اگر قضاء چلانے کے لئے دوسرا آدمی موجود ہے اور اپنے نہ ہونے سے امت کی ہلاکت کا خطرہ نہیں ہے تو اس کی خواہش رکھنا بھی مناسب نہیں اور اس کا مانگنا بھی مناسب نہیں۔ کسی آدمی کا غلط فیصلہ ہو جائے تو اس کا گناہ قاضی کے سر پر ہے۔ اس لئے بلاوجہ اس مصیبت میں پڑنا اچھا نہیں ہے (٢) حدیث میں ہے۔ حدثني عبد الرحمن بن سمرة قال: قال لي رسول الله الا الله يا عبد الرحمن بن سمرة لا تسأل الامارة فان اعطيتها عن مسألة وكلت اليها وان اعطيتها عن غير مسألة اعنت عليها (بخاری شریف، باب من سأل الامارة وكل الیها ص ۱۰۵۸، نمبر ۱۴۷، مسلم شریف، باب انھی عن طلب الامارة والحرص عليها ، ج ٢، ص ۱۲۰ نمبر ۴۷۱۵/۱۶۵٢) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ امارت اور قضا نہیں مانگنا چاہئے (٣) دوسری حدیث میں ہے۔ عن ابی موسی قال : دخلت على النبي الله انا ورجلان من بنی عمى، فقال احد الرجلين يا رسول الله ! امرنا على بعض ما ولاك الله عز وجل، وقال الآخر مثل ذلك. فقال: انا والله لا نولى على هذا العمل احدا سأله ولا احدا حرص عليه (مسلم شریف، باب النھی عن طلب الامارة والحرص علیها ص ۱۲۰، نمبر ۷۳۳، بخاری شریف، باب ما یکرہ من الحرصى على الامارة ص ۱۰۵۸، نمبر ۷۱۴۹ ) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ جو قضاء مانگے یا اس کی حرص کرے اس کو قضاء نہ دی جائے۔
Book of the Conduct of the Judge. [2891](4) It is not appropriate to seek or ask for the office (of qadi). There is a hadith: 'The one who is made a qadi is considered to be slaughtered without a knife.' From this it is known that if one is incapable, taking qada is not good. (2) Another hadith clarifies this: From Abu Dharr that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'O Abu Dharr, I see you are weak, and I love for you what I love for myself. Do not be a commander over two (persons) and do not be a guardian of an orphan's property.' (Muslim, Bab Karahiyat al-Imarah bi Ghayr Darurah, vol. 2, p. 121, no. 1826; Abu Dawud, Bab ma Ja'a fi al-Dukhul fi al-Wasaya, p. 40, no. 2868) This hadith states that if a person is incapable of qada, he should not take qada. (3) The verse above also says that if one follows his desires, he will go astray. Therefore, if one fears incapacity, he should not take qada so as not to go astray. (4) Neither is it appropriate to request the office nor to ask for it. If there is another person available to discharge qada and there is no danger of the ummah's destruction due to one's absence, then it is not appropriate to desire it nor to ask for it. If a person makes a wrong judgment, its sin is upon the qadi. Therefore, without cause, entering into this calamity is not good. (2) There is a hadith: 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Samurah narrated: The Messenger of Allah said to me: O Abd al-Rahman ibn Samurah, do not ask for authority, for if you are given it on account of asking, you will be entrusted to it; and if you are given it without asking, you will be helped in it.' (Bukhari, Bab man Sa'ala al-Imarata wa Kulla ilayha, p. 1058, no. 147; Muslim, Bab al-Nahy 'an Talab al-Imara wa al-Hirs 'alayha, vol. 2, p. 120, no. 4715/1652) From this hadith it is learned that one should not ask for emirate or qada. (3) Another hadith: From Abu Musa: I entered upon the Prophet (peace be upon him) along with two men from my paternal cousins. One of them said: O Messenger of Allah! Appoint us as rulers over some of what Allah has entrusted to you. The other said the same. He said: 'By Allah, I will not appoint to this work anyone who asks for it or anyone who is eager for it.' (Muslim, Bab al-Nahy 'an Talab al-Imara wa al-Hirs 'alayha, p. 120, no. 733; Bukhari, Bab ma Yukrah min al-Hirs 'ala al-Imara, p. 1058, no. 7149) From this hadith it is learned that whoever asks for qada or is eager for it, he should not be given qada.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the prohibition of seeking judicial authority. The author begins by stating that it is not appropriate to seek or ask for the position of qadi. He supports this with three hadith. The first hadith compares becoming a qadi to being slaughtered without a knife, indicating the severity and responsibility. The second hadith, from Abu Dharr, shows that the Prophet prohibited him from being a leader even over two persons or guardian of an orphan's property, implying that one should avoid such positions if weak. The third hadith from Abd al-Rahman ibn Samurah explicitly says not to ask for authority, as asking leads to being left to one's own devices, while being appointed without asking brings divine help. The fourth hadith from Abu Musa shows that the Prophet refused to appoint those who asked or were eager. The author concludes that if one fears incapacity, he should not take qada. The ruling is that seeking judicial authority is not permissible (makruh tanzih) for those who are not fully qualified or when others are available. The Hanafi school emphasizes that the qadi should be competent and appointed without personal desire to avoid bias and sin.

Key Terms
الولاية (al-wilayah): Here it means the position of judge or ruler in general.
الامارة (al-imarah): Leadership or command; synonymous with authority in this context.
القضاء (al-qada): The office of judge; the act of judging.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) It is permissible only if one is appointed without asking.

Key Takeaway
Seeking judicial authority (qada) is discouraged in the Hanafi school due to its heavy responsibility and the Prophet's explicit warnings against asking for power.

Handing Over the Register of the Previous Qadi
Ruling that the new qadi receives the register of the former qadi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling regarding the transfer of the qadi's register and explain its purpose.

Core Idea
The diwan is not personal property—it must be transferred to ensure continuity of justice.

A new qadi inherits not only the authority but also the records of the previous qadi. This ruling clarifies that the diwan—the official register—must be handed over so that the new qadi can properly adjudicate pending and future cases. It underscores the importance of judicial continuity and record-keeping in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the handing over of the previous qadi's register to the new qadi. According to the Hanafi school, this is a procedural requirement; the ruling is mubah (permissible) and recommended (mustahabb).
Not a religious obligation but a practical necessity.
2
State the purpose
The purpose is to enable the new qadi to examine the register and fulfill the rights of those entitled—ensuring ongoing cases and judgments are properly addressed.
Justice requires knowledge of past decisions.
3
Trace the authority
This practice is implied in the conduct of the early caliphs and is considered part of the established tradition (zahir al-riwayah). Later Hanafi scholars (fatawa mutakhkhirin) affirm it as the mufta bihi position.
No intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf reported.
4
Recognize modern relevance
Today, this ruling maps to the transfer of case files, electronic records, and court documents when a judge is replaced. It emphasizes that official records are public trust.
Personal notes are not included.

Source Text

This is a ruling from the chapter on the conduct of the judge. It states that when a new qadi is appointed, the previous qadi's official register (diwan) should be handed over to him. The purpose is to ensure continuity in adjudicating cases and delivering rights. The register contains records of judgments, debts, and other matters. The new qadi must study it to properly execute justice.

Key Terms

The Qadi's Duty to Examine Prisoners and Enforce Confessions
Ruling that a new qadi must enforce claims based on prisoners' confessions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the qadi's duty regarding prisoners and apply the rule that a confession is binding without additional evidence.

Core Idea
A prisoner's confession is binding proof; the qadi must enforce it without further evidence.

A new qadi inherits unresolved cases, including imprisoned persons. This ruling clarifies that if a prisoner voluntarily admits a debt or claim, the qadi must enforce it immediately. The confession alone suffices; no witnesses are needed. This ensures justice is swift and relies on the individual's own admission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the duty of the qadi
What is the qadi supposed to do with prisoners? The first phrase says 'وینظر في حال المحبوسین' – he examines their condition.
This is an obligation.
2
Determine the trigger for enforcement
The ruling applies when a prisoner confesses to a claim. Find the phrase 'فمن اعترف منهم بحق' – whoever confesses to a claim.
Confession is the key event.
3
Understand the evidentiary value of confession
The commentary explains that confession alone is sufficient; no witnesses are needed. Why? Because it is an admission against oneself.
This is a principle of Hanafi law.
4
Analyze the supporting athar
The athar from Shuraih shows that even if the confessor later denies, the initial confession is binding.
This reinforces the conclusiveness of confession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[2893] (٦) وينظر في حال المحبوسين فمن اعترف منهم بحق ألزمه اياه. قاضی بننے کے بعد وہ قیدیوں کے حالات کا معائنہ کرے ۔ جو قیدی اعتراف کرے کہ مجھ پر فلاں کا حق ہے تو اس پر وہ حق لازم کردے۔ جب قیدی نے خود اعتراف کر لیا کہ مجھ پر فلاں کا حق ہے تو اب گواہی کی بھی ضرورت نہیں ہے اس کا اقرار کرنا کافی ہے۔ اس لئے اس پر فلاں کا حق لازم کر دیا جائے گا۔ اس اثر میں ہے۔ عن ابن سیرین قال اعترف رجل عند شریح بامر ثم انكره فقضى عليه باعترافه (مصنف عبدالرزاق، باب الاعتراف عند القاضی ، ج ثامن ، ص ۱۳۰۳، نمبر ( ۱۵٣٠))
[2893] (6) And he examines the condition of those who are imprisoned. So whoever among them confesses to a claim, he enforces it against him. After becoming qadi, he inspects the condition of the prisoners. If a prisoner confesses that someone has a right against him, he makes that right binding on him. When the prisoner himself confesses that so-and-so has a right against him, then there is no need for witnesses; his admission is sufficient. Therefore, the right will be made binding on him. There is an athar: Ibn Sirin said: A person made a confession before Shuraih of a matter, then denied it, so Shuraih judged against him on the basis of his confession. (Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, Bab al-I'tiraf 'inda al-Qadi, vol. 8, p. 1303, no. 1530)
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This ruling instructs the new qadi to review the cases of prisoners. The key point is that if a prisoner acknowledges a debt or claim against himself, the qadi must enforce that claim based on the confession alone—no witnesses are needed. The commentary supports this with an athar from Shuraih al-Qadi, who ruled based on a confession despite a later denial. The principle is that a confession is binding in Hanafi law. The qadi does not need additional evidence after a valid confession.

Key Terms
الاعتراف (al-i'tiraf): The act of admitting a claim or debt against oneself; it is a binding proof in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A voluntary confession is a complete proof; the qadi must enforce it without further investigation.

The Testimony of a Removed Qadi: Ordinary Person After Removal
Ruling: removed qadi's statement not accepted without witnesses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a removed qadi's claim and explain the reasoning based on the athar of Umar (RA).

Core Idea
A removed qadi is a private individual; his word carries no special weight without witnesses.

This lesson addresses a specific procedural rule in qada: when a prisoner denies a claim, the statement of a former qadi (now removed) is not accepted as evidence unless supported by witnesses. The underlying principle is that removal from office returns the qadi to the status of an ordinary layperson. Let's explore the ruling, its proof, and its practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Scope
The ruling: if a defendant denies the claim, the removed qadi's statement is not accepted without witnesses (bayyinah). The scope is limited to situations where the qadi is no longer in office.
This does not apply to a sitting qadi; his official record may have different weight.
2
Understand the Underlying Principle
After removal, the qadi becomes a common person (aam admi). His testimony is no different from any other Muslim's. This is the key shift: removal strips the qadi of judicial authority.
The principle is derived from the general rule that authority is tied to office, not person.
3
Examine the Dalil (Athar)
The text cites an athar from Umar (RA) to Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf: even while sitting as amir, his testimony is like that of a common Muslim. This implies that after removal, it is even more so.
The athar is recorded in Bukhari (no. 7170).
4
Analyse the Wajh al-Dalalah
How does the athar prove the ruling? Umar's statement establishes that no special status (like amirate) elevates one's testimony. By analogy (qiyas), a removed qadi has no special status either.
The Hanafi school uses this athar as a qawl sahabi, which is a valid source.
5
Note Cross-Madhhab Agreement
This ruling is mutually agreed upon (muttafaq alayh) among the schools; no ikhtilaf is reported.
The agreement strengthens the ruling's reliability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[2894] (٧) ومن انكر لم يقبل قول المعزول عليه الا ببينة. اور اگر کسی نے انکار کیا تو معزول قاضی کا قول مقبول نہیں ہے مگر گواہی کے ساتھ۔ یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ قاضی معزول ہونے کے بعد ایک عام آدمی ہو گیا اب وہ قاضی نہیں رہا اس لئے جس طرح عام آدمیوں کی بات گواہی کے ذریعہ قبول کی جاتی ہے اسی طرح معزول قاضی کی بات بھی گواہی کے ذریعہ قبول کی جائے گی۔ اثر میں ہے کہ قاضی کے عہدے پر ہوتے ہوئے بھی ایک عام آدمی کی شہادت کی طرح ان کی شہادت ہے تو معزول ہونے کے بعد بدرجہ اولی ایک عام آدمی کی طرح ہو جائے گا۔ اثر میں ہے۔ قال عمر لعبد الرحمن بن عوف لو رأيت رجلا على حد زنا او سرقة وانت امير ؟ فقال شهادتك شهادة رجل من المسلمين قال صدقت (بخاری شریف، باب الشهادة تكون عند الحاكم في ولاية القضاء، ص ۱۰۶٢ نمبر ۷۱۷۰)
[2894] (7) And whoever denies (the claim), the word of the removed qadi is not accepted against him except with evidence (witnesses). This issue is based on the principle that after being removed, the qadi becomes a common person; he is no longer a qadi. Therefore, just as the statement of common people is accepted through testimony, similarly the statement of the removed qadi will be accepted through testimony. There is an athar: While he was in office as qadi, his testimony is like the testimony of a common Muslim; then after removal, it is even more so that he becomes like a common person. There is an athar: Umar said to Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf: 'If you see a man committing a hadd crime like zina or theft while you are an amir?' He said: 'Your testimony is like the testimony of a Muslim man.' He (Umar) said: 'You have spoken the truth.' (Bukhari, Bab al-Shahada Takun 'inda al-Hakim fi Wilayat al-Qada, p. 1062, no. 7170)
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This ruling addresses the situation where a prisoner denies the claim. The removed (former) qadi cannot have his statement accepted unless it is supported by witnesses. The reasoning is that after removal, the ex-qadi is a private individual; his testimony is treated like any other layperson's. This is based on an athar from Umar (RA) indicating that even a sitting ruler's testimony is like that of an ordinary Muslim. Hence, the removed qadi's word requires corroboration.

Key Terms
المعزول (al-ma'zul): The qadi who has been removed from office.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the statement of a removed qadi not accepted without witnesses?
A) Because he lost his memory.
B) Because after removal, he becomes like a common person and his testimony is not special.
C) Because he is untrustworthy.
D) Because the qadi's testimony is never accepted.

Key Takeaway
A removed qadi's claim must be supported by witnesses because removal restores him to the status of an ordinary Muslim.

Procedure for Releasing a Prisoner Without Evidence
Ruling that a prisoner without evidence is not released hastily but after public notice

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on releasing a prisoner without evidence and explain the required procedure of public announcement and investigation.

Core Idea
A qadi must not hastily release a prisoner even without evidence; due process requires public notice and careful investigation.

This lesson addresses a critical procedural safeguard in Islamic judiciary. When a prisoner denies a claim and no evidence is presented, the qadi must not immediately set him free. Instead, he must make a public announcement and thoroughly investigate before release. This ruling ensures that all potential claims are considered and that justice is fully served.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Determine the legal classification (hukm) of the qadi's action: it is wajib (obligatory) not to hasten release.
The passage uses 'lam yu'ajjal' (do not hasten), indicating a prohibition of immediate release.
2
Identify the Required Actions
List the two actions the qadi must perform: (1) make a public proclamation (nida), (2) thoroughly investigate (istizhar).
These are sequential: first announce, then investigate.
3
Understand the Wisdom
Why is this procedure required? To protect against premature release that might deprive others of their rights (e.g., other claimants).
This reflects the Hanafi emphasis on caution (ihtiyat) in judicial matters.
4
Apply the Ruling
Determine the scenario: applies only when the prisoner denies and no evidence is brought by the plaintiff.
If the prisoner admits, or evidence exists, this procedure is not needed.
5
Note the Precaution
Recognize that this ruling is a precautionary measure to ensure no rights are lost, even if it delays release.
The qadi acts as guardian of rights, not merely a dispute resolver.

Inspecting Prisoners and Acting on Confessions
Ruling on examining prisoners and enforcing confessions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the judge's duty regarding prisoners and apply the rule that a confession alone is binding.

Core Idea
A confession against oneself is conclusive and must be enforced immediately.

When a judge takes office, he must inspect the prison and examine each prisoner's case. If a prisoner admits to a debt or right, the judge must enforce it based solely on that confession. This lesson explains the Hanafi position that a confession is binding even if later retracted, supported by the practice of the early judge Shuraih.

Guided Path
1
Identify the judge's duty
Read the first phrase 'وينظر في حال المحبوسين' and understand that the judge is required to inspect prisoners.
2
Determine the consequence of a confession
Analyze 'فمن اعترف منهم بحق ألزمه إياه' — the judge enforces the confessed right without further investigation.
The Arabic 'ألزمه' means he makes it obligatory, i.e., enforces.
3
Examine the evidential proof (athar)
Read the athar from Ibn Sirin: a man confessed before Shuraih, then denied, yet Shuraih judged against him based on the initial confession.
This shows that retraction does not invalidate a confession.
4
Understand the underlying principle
Confession (iqrar) is binding because a person's own admission against themselves is accepted as truth in Hanafi law.
No additional witnesses are needed.
5
Apply to a similar scenario
Imagine a prisoner acknowledges a debt. The judge must order payment immediately, even if the prisoner later changes his story.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَينظر في حال المحبوسين فمن اعترف منهم بحق ألزمه إياه. وفي الأثر عن ابن سيرين قال: اعترف رجل عند شريح بأمر ثم أنكره فقضى عليه باعترافه.
And he looks into the situation of the imprisoned ones; so whoever among them confesses to a right, he obligates it upon him. And in the athar from Ibn Sirin: A man confessed to something before Shuraih, then denied it, and he (Shuraih) judged against him based on his confession.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This lesson discusses the judge's duty regarding prisoners. Upon assuming office, the judge must inspect the prison and examine each prisoner's case. If a prisoner admits to a debt or a right owed to another, the judge must enforce that right immediately based on the confession alone; no further evidence is required. This is because a person's own admission against himself is conclusive in Hanafi law. The text supports this with the practice of Shuraih, who upheld the initial confession even when the person later retracted it. The principle is that a confession (iqrar) is binding and cannot be withdrawn unless proven to be false. This ruling is from the Adab al-Qadi section, highlighting the judge's proactive role.

Key Terms

Evidentiary Value of a Deposited Judge's Statement
Ruling on rejecting the deposed judge's claim without evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that a deposed judge's claim against a denying prisoner requires evidence (bayyinah) and apply it to a scenario.

Core Idea
A deposed judge's claim against an accused is not accepted without evidence — he is no different from an ordinary Muslim.

This lesson examines the evidentiary weight of a former judge's word. The Hanafi school maintains that once a judge is removed, his testimony or claim is treated like that of any layperson. The ruling is supported by a precedent from Umar ibn al-Khattab, who emphasized that even a governor's testimony is ordinary. This protects the accused from arbitrary detention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
State the clear ruling: the statement of a deposed judge (ma'zul) against a denier is not accepted without evidence (bayyinah).
2
Understand the Basis
Analyze the precedent: Umar's question to Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf establishes that even a governor's testimony is like any Muslim's. By analogy, a deposed judge is even less privileged, so his word is ordinary.
The proof is a Qawl Sahabi (saying of a companion), which is a valid source in Hanafi usul but zanni in transmission.
3
Determine the Evidentiary Requirement
Conclude that the deposed judge must present bayyinah (typically two male witnesses) to support his claim, just like any plaintiff. If he fails, the claim is dismissed.
This is a procedural obligation (wajib) on the judge to require evidence.
4
Recognize the Protection
Note that this rule protects the accused from being held on mere assertion by a former authority, upholding the principle that innocence is presumed until proven by credible evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن أنكر لم يقبل قول المعزول عليه إلا ببينة. عن عمر لعبد الرحمن بن عوف: لو رأيت رجلا على حد زنا أو سرقة وأنت أمير؟ فقال: شهادتك شهادة رجل من المسلمين. قال صدقت.
And whoever denies, the statement of the deposed (judge) against him is not accepted except with evidence. From Umar to Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf: 'If you see a man committing hudud for zina or theft while you are an ameer?' He said: 'Your testimony is the testimony of a man from the Muslims.' He said: 'You have spoken the truth.'
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This lesson explains the evidentiary value of a deposed judge's statement. Once a judge is removed from office, he becomes an ordinary person. If a prisoner denies the allegation, the former judge's word cannot be taken as decisive; rather, he must provide valid witnesses like any other plaintiff. The text cites the precedent of Umar ibn al-Khattab, who told Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf that even while in authority, his testimony counts as that of any Muslim man—so after removal, it is certainly ordinary. The Hanafi ruling is that the former judge's claim requires evidence (bayyinah) to be accepted. This protects the accused from arbitrary detention based on unsubstantiated claims.

Key Terms
المعزول (al-ma'zul): The judge who has been removed from office.
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence, typically two male witnesses or one male and two females.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) His own statement
B) Confession of the prisoner
C) Evidence (bayyinah)
D) Oath of the judge

Key Takeaway
A deposed judge's claim against a denier requires evidence (bayyinah); his word alone has no special evidentiary force.

Procedure When No Evidence: Announce and Investigate
Ruling on not hastily releasing a prisoner without evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when no evidence is established against a prisoner and explain the rationale for delay and investigation.

Core Idea
A judge must not hastily release a prisoner; he must announce and investigate even without evidence.

This lesson addresses a critical moment in judicial procedure: when a prisoner claims innocence but no evidence is produced. The Hanafi school provides clear guidance: the judge must not rush to release. Instead, he must publicly announce the case and actively investigate. This precaution (istiẓhār) balances individual liberty with the need for justice, ensuring that hidden witnesses or evidence are not overlooked.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage applies when evidence (bayyinah) is not established against the prisoner. This is the condition triggering the ruling.
Bayyinah here refers to any legal proof that would justify the imprisonment.
2
Identify the prohibited action
The judge is forbidden from hastening the release (takhliyah). The phrase 'lam yu'ajjal' indicates prohibition or strong discouragement (wujub of delay).
Hastening release could lead to injustice if the prisoner is actually guilty.
3
Identify the required actions
Two obligations: (1) make a public announcement (nida') calling for witnesses or information; (2) investigate thoroughly (yastazhir) by seeking evidence.
Istiẓhār implies proactive searching, not passive waiting.
4
Understand the rationale
The prisoner may have been imprisoned correctly by the previous judge; distant witnesses may exist. The delay allows them to come forward.
This precaution prevents premature release of a guilty person.
5
Note the Hanafi approach
Hanafi fiqh emphasizes caution (ihtiyat) in matters of justice, preferring to delay release when doubt exists.
Other schools may differ, but Hanafi requires explicit procedure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن لم تقم البينة لم يُعجّل بتخليته حتى ينادى عليه ويستظهر في أمره.
And if the evidence is not established, he should not hasten to release him until a call is made for him and investigation is undertaken regarding his matter.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This lesson deals with the procedure when a prisoner claims innocence but no evidence is available. The judge should not immediately release him; rather, he should announce publicly (call for witnesses) and thoroughly investigate. The reason is that the deposed judge likely had a valid reason for imprisonment. The Hanafi approach balances individual liberty with the need for justice. The judge waits for possible distant witnesses to come forward. This shows the precaution (istizhar) required before releasing a prisoner.

Key Terms
تخليته (takhliyatuhu): His release from prison.
يستظهر (yastazhir): He seeks clarity or investigates thoroughly.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Release him immediately
B) Keep him indefinitely
C) Announce and investigate before releasing
D) Punish him anyway

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi qāḍī must exercise precaution (istiẓhār) before releasing a prisoner, ensuring all possible evidence is gathered.

The Judge's Duty in Trusts and Waqf Revenues
Ruling on overseeing trusts and waqf incomes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the judge's duty regarding trusts and waqf revenues and identify the two bases on which he acts.

Core Idea
The judge acts on evidence or admission in managing trusts and waqf incomes.

This short ruling from Adab al-Qadi outlines a key administrative duty of a judge. He must oversee trusts and waqf revenues, ensuring they reach rightful beneficiaries. The decision is based solely on established evidence or the possessor's own admission. This prevents unnecessary litigation and ensures property is correctly managed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objects of oversight
The judge must look into two types of property: trusts (wada'i') and the revenues of endowments (irtifa' al-wuquf).
These are assets held in trust for specific beneficiaries.
2
Determine the sources of action
The judge decides based on either clear evidence (bayyinah) or the admission (i'tiraf) of the person who currently possesses the property.
Admission is sufficient even without witnesses.
3
Recognize the legal obligation
This duty is wajib (obligatory) on the judge, not merely recommended.
4
Apply to a scenario
If a custodian of a waqf admits that the rent collected belongs to the waqf, the judge accepts that and directs the funds accordingly.
No further proof needed if admission is clear.

He acts based on either clear evidence (bayyinah) or the admission of the person who currently possesses the property. If the possessor says, 'This belongs to so-and-so,' that is accepted. This is a practical rule to resolve property claims without litigation.

Accepting the Deposited Judge's Statement
Ruling on the deposed judge's statement regarding property possession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for accepting a deposed judge's statement regarding property and apply it to scenarios in qada.

Core Idea
A deposed judge's claim alone is not proof; only the possessor's admission gives it weight.

In judicial proceedings, a deposed judge may make claims about property he previously handled. However, Hanafi qada does not take his word alone—it requires confirmation from the current possessor. This lesson clarifies that crucial condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default rule
The text begins with a general prohibition: the statement of the deposed judge is not accepted. Recognize that this is the baseline position.
2
Locate the exception
The phrase 'إلا أن يعترف الذي هو في يده' introduces the sole exception: if the current possessor admits that the deposed handed the property to him. That admission makes the deposed's statement acceptable.
The admission is binding against the possessor himself.
3
Understand the rationale
The possessor's admission is reliable because it is against his own interest (iqrar against self is legally valid). This indirectly confirms the deposed's action without needing direct evidence.
4
Consider the consequence of denial
If the possessor denies, the deposed's statement remains unaccepted, and the property stays with the possessor unless other evidence appears.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يقبل قول المعزول إلا أن يعترف الذي هو في يده أن المعزول سلمها إليه فيقبل قوله فيها.
And the statement of the deposed (judge) is not accepted unless the one in whose possession it is admits that the deposed handed it over to him; then his statement regarding it is accepted.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This ruling clarifies that the deposed judge's claim about a property being in his possession or belonging to someone is not accepted on its own. However, if the current possessor during the new judge's inquiry states, 'The deposed judge gave this property to me,' then his statement is accepted as a valid admission. The rationale is that the possessor's admission against himself is reliable, and it indirectly confirms the deposed's action.

Key Terms
المعزول (al-ma'zul): The deposed judge; a judge removed from office.
اعترف (i'tarafa): Admitted; confessed; acknowledged.
سلمها إليه (sallamaha ilayhi): He handed it over to him; delivered the property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Always
B) When supported by two witnesses
C) When the current possessor admits receiving it from him
D) When the property is waqf

Key Takeaway
A deposed judge's claim is not proof; it becomes admissible only when confirmed by the possessor's own admission.

Examining Deposits and Waqf Revenues: Evidence or Confession
Ruling on the judge's duty to investigate deposits and waqf revenues

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two legal bases (bayyinah and iqrar) upon which a judge must act regarding deposits and waqf revenues, and apply this rule to a given scenario.

Core Idea
A judge decides on deposits and waqf income only by evidence or confession, not assumption.

This passage from Adab al-Qadi instructs the incoming judge on how to handle cases of deposits and waqf revenues. It establishes a clear procedure: act only on established evidence or the possessor's admission. We'll examine the reasoning behind this restriction and its practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of examination
The judge is commanded to look into deposits (wada'i') and waqf revenues (irtifa' al-wuquf). Determine what these are and why they require scrutiny.
Deposits are trusts left with the previous judge; waqf revenues are income from endowments.
2
Extract the two legal bases for action
The text explicitly states that the judge acts according to what is established by evidence (bayyinah) or confessed by the possessor (iqrar). List these two bases.
No other basis is mentioned, implying exclusivity.
3
Understand the rationale for the limitation
Consider why the judge cannot act on mere suspicion, personal knowledge, or hearsay. The Shariah requires certainty in property matters, and only bayyinah or iqrar provide that certainty.
This protects the rights of all parties.
4
Apply to a practical scenario
Given a scenario (e.g., a claimant appears, the holder makes a statement), decide whether the judge should act based on the two bases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
امره [۲۸۹۲](۹) وينظر فى الودائع وارتفاع الوقوف فيعمل على حسب ماتقوم به البيئة او يعترف به من هو في يده

نئے قاضی کا کام یہ بھی ہے کہ رکھی ہوئی امانت کی چیزوں میں غور کرے کہ کس کی ہے۔ جس کے متعلق بینہ قائم ہوامانت کا مال حقدار تک پہنچائے۔ یا جس کے ہاتھ میں امانت ہو وہ اقرار کرے کہ یہ امانت فلاں کی ہے تو فلاں کو وہ امانت دلوائی جائے۔ اسی طرح وقف کی آمدنی میں غور کرے کہ کتنی آمدنی آرہی ہے اور کہاں کہاں خرچ ہو رہی ہے۔ اس سلسلے میں بھی جس بات پر بعینہ قائم ہو اس کو پورا کرے۔ یا جو اقرار کرے کہ یہ چیز میرے ہاتھ میں ہے اور فلاں کی ہے اس کے مطابق عمل کرے۔ کوئی فیصلہ کرنے کے لئے دو ہی صورتیں ہیں یا اس پر بینہ قائم ہو یا قبضہ والا اس کا اقرار کرے کہ یہ فلاں کا ہے۔

ودائع : ودیعہ کی جمع ہے امانت - ارتفاع : آمدنی ، ارتفاع رفع سے مشتق ہے اٹھنا۔ یہاں آمدنی مراد ہے۔
His command: [2892](9) And he should look into the deposits and the revenues of the waqf, then act according to what is established by evidence or what is confessed by the one in whose possession it is. Also: It is also the duty of the new judge to consider the deposited items, to determine whose they are. He should deliver the deposit to the rightful owner if evidence is established, or if the possessor confesses that it belongs to so-and-so. Similarly, he should consider the revenues of the waqf, how much is coming in and where it is being spent. In this regard, he should implement whatever is clearly established, or act according to the confession that it is in my hand and belongs to so-and-so. There are only two ways to decide: either evidence is established, or the possessor confesses that it belongs to so-and-so. Deposits (wada'i') is the plural of wadi'ah (trust). Revenues (irtifa') is derived from raf' (to rise), meaning income.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the book of Adab al-Qadi. The new judge must examine deposits and waqf revenues. He must act based on either clear evidence (bayyinah) or the confession of the person who has possession. The Urdu commentary explains that the judge should investigate to whom deposits belong. If evidence points to someone, he delivers it. If the possessor confesses, he acts accordingly. Similarly for waqf income. The two modes of decision are evidence or confession. This is a straightforward ruling without proof.

Key Terms
ودائع (wada'i'): Plural of wadi'ah (trust); items deposited with the previous judge.
ارتفاع الوقوف (irtifa' al-wuquf): Revenues or income generated by endowments (awqaf).
بینه (bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically two witnesses or a recognized proof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Evidence and oath
B) Evidence and confession of the possessor
C) Confession and presumption
D) Oath and presumption

Key Takeaway
A judge's decisions regarding deposits and waqf revenues are limited to two valid bases: established evidence (bayyinah) and the possessor's confession (iqrar).

The Statement of a Dismissed Judge is Not Accepted
Ruling on the admissibility of a dismissed judge's statement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the admissibility of a dismissed judge's claim about deposits and identify the sole condition under which it becomes acceptable.

Core Idea
A dismissed judge's word is not accepted; only the current possessor's confession can validate his claim.

This lesson examines a specific rule in Islamic judicial procedure: once a judge is removed from office, his statements about deposits or properties from his tenure carry no legal weight. The Hanafi school treats him as an ordinary person, so his claims are not automatically accepted. However, if the person currently holding the property confirms that the dismissed judge had entrusted it to him, then the statement becomes admissible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
Recognize that the default rule is: the statement of a dismissed judge regarding deposits is not accepted. This means it has no legal effect as evidence.
2
Identify the exception
Note the exception: the statement is accepted if the person in possession confesses that the dismissed judge handed over the item to him.
The exception requires an active admission from the possessor.
3
Understand the rationale
The rationale is that after dismissal, the judge becomes like any other individual. His authority is gone, so his word alone does not carry evidentiary weight. The possession of another is trusted unless the possessor himself acknowledges the former judge's claim.
This prevents former officials from making unsupported claims against current possessors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۲۸۹۷) (۱۰) ولا يقبل قول المعزول الا ان يعترف الذي هو في يده ان المعزول سلمها اليه فيقبل قوله فيها

پہلے گزر چکا ہے کہ معزول قاضی ایک عام آدمی کی طرح ہو گیا اس لئے اس کی بات اس وقت تک نہیں مانی جائے گی جب تک کہ قبضہ والا یہ نہ کہے کہ مجھے معزول قاضی نے یہ چیز دی تھی۔
(2897)(10) And the statement of the dismissed judge is not accepted unless the one in whose possession it is confesses that the dismissed judge handed it over to him, then his statement regarding it is accepted. Previously it has passed that the dismissed judge becomes like a common person, so his word will not be accepted until the possessor says that the dismissed judge gave this thing to me.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This is a ruling regarding the credibility of a dismissed judge. Once a judge is removed, he is like any ordinary person. His claims about deposits or properties from his previous tenure are not accepted unless the person currently in possession confirms that the dismissed judge had indeed entrusted it to him. This prevents misuse of authority and ensures that property rights are based on current possession and evidence.

Key Terms
المعزول (al-ma'zul): The dismissed judge, whose judicial authority is terminated.
قول (qawl): Statement or claim; in legal context, an assertion that may be accepted or rejected as evidence.

Sitting for Judgment in the Mosque: Hanafi Position and Shafi'i Objection
Ruling and inter-madhhab dispute on permissibility of judge sitting in mosque.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on a judge sitting in the mosque for judgment, cite the hadith evidence, and explain the Shafi'i objection and the Hanafi response.

Core Idea
The judge may sit in the mosque for judgment, but must ensure accessibility for all litigants.

The placement of a judge's seat may seem like a minor procedural detail, but it reflects deeper legal principles: the sanctity of the mosque, the need for public access to justice, and the role of evidence in settling juristic differences. This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling that a judge may sit in the mosque for judgment, the Shafi'i objection, and how the Hanafi school responds while upholding both the spirit of the sunnah and practical needs.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: the judge should sit in a conspicuous place in the mosque so that all litigants can easily approach. This is considered permissible (ja'iz) based on prophetic practice.
The text emphasizes 'zahiran' (conspicuous) to ensure accessibility.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i opposes this practice. His objection is based on the exclusion of impure individuals (polytheists) and menstruating/postpartum women from the mosque.
The Shafi'i concern is about ritual purity and the mosque's sanctity.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Shafi'i argues: 'Polytheists are impure (Quran 9:28) and cannot enter the mosque; similarly, menstruating women are forbidden. Therefore, holding court there would exclude some litigants.'
The objection is practical and based on Quranic and fiqh principles.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response: Hadith evidence (adulterer's confession and li'an) proves the Prophet judged in the mosque. However, the judge should come out for those who cannot enter. This balances the sunnah with inclusivity.
A hadith about not closing the door supports openness.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafis allow the judge to sit in the mosque as a norm, but require accommodation for excluded persons. Shafi'is prefer the judge to sit outside to avoid the issue entirely.
Both aim for justice, but differ on starting default.

Source Text

(۲۸۹۸) (۱۱) ويجلس للحكم جلوسا ظاهرا في المسجد

حاکم مسجد میں ایسی ظاہری جگہ پر فیصلے کے لئے بیٹھے جس سے ہر آنے والے کو پتا چل جائے کہ یہ قاضی صاحب بیٹھے ہیں۔ اور وہاں ہر خص آسانی سے آسکے۔

حضور نے زنا کا فیصلہ مسجد میں کیا ہے۔ جس سے معلوم ہوا کہ فیصلے کے لئے مسجد میں بیٹھ سکتا ہے۔ حدیث یہ ہے۔ عن ابی ھریرة قال: اتـي رجـل رسول الله الا الله وهو في المسجد فناداه فقال : يا رسول الله ! انی زنیت فاعرض عنه الخ (الف) ( بخاری شریف، باب من حکم فی المسجد الخ، ص ۱۰۶۲، نمبر ۷۱۶۷ر مسلم شریف، باب من اعترف على نفسه بالزنی ص ۶۶ نمبر ۱۹۹۲) (۲) دوسری حدیث میں ہے۔ عن سهل اخی بنی ساعدة ان رجلا من الانصار جاء الى النبي الله فقال ارأيت رجلا وجد مع امرأته رجلا ايقتله ؟ فتلاعنا في المسجد وانا شاهد (ب) ( بخاری شریف، باب من قضی ولائن فی المسجد، ص ۱۰۶۲، نمبر ۷۱۶۶ )
ان احادیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ مسجد میں فیصلہ کیا جا سکتا ہے۔
امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ مسجد میں فیصلے کے لئے نہ بیٹھے۔
وہاں فیصلے کے لئے مشرک آئیں گے جو نجس ہیں وہ مسجد میں کیسے داخل ہوں گے۔ حائضہ اور نفساء عورتیں فیصلے کے لئے آئیں گی جو مسجد میں داخل نہیں ہو سکتیں۔ اس لئے مسجد میں فیصلے کے لئے نہ بیٹھے۔ ہم کہتے ہیں کہ حدیث ہے اس لئے بیٹھ سکتے ہیں البتہ ایسے لوگوں کے لئے قاضی باہر آجائے۔ اور ایسی جگہ بیٹھے جہاں ہر آدمی آسانی سے فیصلے کے لئے پہنچ سکے اس کی دلیل یہ حدیث ہے۔ قال عمرو بن مرة لمعاوية اني سمعت رسول الله الله يقول : ما من امام يغلق بابه دون ذوى الحاجة والخلة والمسكنة الا اغلق الله ابواب السماء دون خلته وحاجته و مسکنته (ج) ( ترمذی شریف، باب ماجاء فی امام الرعیة، ص ۱۲۴۸، نمبر ۱۳۳۲) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ فیصلے چاہنے والوں کے لئے ظاہر مقام پر بیٹھے تا کہ فیصلہ چاہنے میں رکاوٹ نہ ہو۔
[2898] (11) And he should sit for judgment in a conspicuous place in the mosque. The judge should sit in a prominent place in the mosque for judgment, so that everyone who comes can know that the judge is sitting, and every opponent can easily approach him. The Prophet (peace be upon him) judged the case of adultery in the mosque. This shows that one can sit in the mosque for judgment. The hadith: from Abu Huraira that a man came to the Messenger of Allah while he was in the mosque and called out: O Messenger of Allah! I have committed adultery, so he turned away from him... (Bukhari, Muslim). Another hadith: from Sahl ibn Sa'd that a man from the Ansar came to the Prophet and said: Do you think if a man finds another man with his wife, should he kill him? Then they performed li'an in the mosque, and I was a witness (Bukhari). These hadiths show that judgment can be conducted in the mosque. Imam Shafi'i says: He should not sit for judgment in the mosque. Polytheists will come for judgment, and they are impure, how can they enter the mosque? Menstruating and postpartum women will come for judgment, they cannot enter the mosque. Therefore, do not sit in the mosque for judgment. We (Hanafis) say that there are hadiths, so he may sit, but for such people, the judge should come outside and sit in a place where every person can easily reach for judgment. The proof is the hadith of Amr ibn Murrah: I heard the Messenger of Allah say: No leader closes his door to the needy, the poor, and the destitute, but Allah closes the gates of heaven to his need, poverty, and destitution (Tirmidhi). This hadith shows that the judge should sit in an open place so that there is no obstruction for those seeking judgment.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the judge sitting in the mosque for judgment. The Hanafi position, based on hadith (the man confessing adultery and the li'an case), allows it. The Urdu commentary elaborates that the judge should sit in a prominent place. Imam Shafi'i objects: polytheists are impure, menstruating women cannot enter, so it is not appropriate. The Hanafi response: hadith permits it, but the judge can come out for such individuals. They also cite a hadith about not closing the door. The relied-upon Hanafi position is that it is permissible to sit in the mosque, but practical considerations may require the judge to sit elsewhere for accessibility.

Key Terms
ظاهرا في المسجد (zahiran fi al-masjid): In a conspicuous place in the mosque, so that all can see.
اللعان (al-li'an): The mutual imprecation between husband and wife when the husband accuses his wife of adultery without witnesses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The mosque is not a suitable place for serious matters
B) Polytheists and menstruating women cannot enter the mosque
C) The judge might be disturbed during prayer
D) The hadith are weak

Key Takeaway
The judge may sit in the mosque for judgment, but must ensure accessibility for all, including those who cannot enter the mosque.

Acceptance of Gifts by the Judge
Ruling on permissible gifts for a judge with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify from whom a Hanafi judge may accept gifts and apply the condition of suspicion to determine permissibility.

Core Idea
A judge's gift is permissible only from mahram or pre-judgeship habit; any suspicious gift is bribery and prohibited.

This lesson presents the Hanafi ruling on gifts to a judge. The author outlines two exceptional categories and warns against bribery. The hadith of Ibn al-Utbiyyah provides the proof, showing the Prophet's rejection of a gift given to a collector.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a judge accepting a gift. The ruling is that it is prohibited (makruh tahrimi) except from two categories: mahram relatives or those who habitually gave gifts before his appointment.
The prohibition is to prevent bribery and partiality.
2
Locate the dalil
The dalil is the hadith of Ibn al-Utbiyyah (from Abu Humaid al-Sa'idi), where the Prophet criticized an official who kept a gift given while on duty.
This is a hadith ahad, but accepted as evidence in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's disapproval shows that gifts given to an official due to his position are impermissible because they create a conflict of interest. The 'illah (effective cause) is the suspicion of bribery.
The ruling applies specifically to judges and similar public officials.
4
Identify the exceptions
Two categories are exempted: (1) mahram relatives (marriage permanently prohibited), as their gifts are seen as familial; (2) those who gave regularly before judgeship, as the gift is not tied to the office.
Even these are conditional: if there is suspicion of bribery, the gift is prohibited.

ہدیہ میں رشوت کا بھی خطرہ ہے کہ ہدیہ دیگر غلط فیصلہ نہ کر والے۔ اس لئے قاضی ذی رحم محرم سے ہدیہ قبول کرے یا قاضی بنے سے پہلے جن لوگوں کی عادت تھی کہ وہ ہدیہ دیا کرتے تھے انہیں لوگوں سے ہدیہ قبول کرے۔ اور اس کا بھی خیال رکھے کہ وہ لوگ بھی کہیں رشوت کے لئے ہدیہ نہیں دے رہے ہوں۔ اگر ایسا ہو تو ان کا ہدیہ بھی قبول نہ کرے۔ ایسا ہدیہ جس میں رشوت کا شبہ ہو اس کو لینے سے حدیث میں منع فرمایا ہے۔ اخبرنا ابو حميد الساعدي قال استعمل النبي رجلا من بنی اسد يقال له ابن الاتبية على صدقة . فلما قدم قال : هذا لكم وهذاا هدى لى فقام النبي الله على حاشیہ : (الف) ایک آدمی حضور کے پاس آیا ، آپ مسجد میں تشریف رکھتے تھے ۔ اس نے آواز دے کر کہا یا رسول اللہ میں نے زنا کیا، پس آپ نے منہ پھیر لیا (ب) انصار کا ایک آدمی حضور کے پاس آیا اور پوچھا کہ کوئی آدمی اپنی بیوی کے ساتھ کسی کو دیکھے تو اس کوقتل نہ کر دے؟ پھر میاں بیوی دونوں نے مسجد میں لعان کیا اور میں وہاں حاضر تھا (ج) حضور فرمایا کرتے تھے کوئی امام ضرورت والے اور مسکین پر اپنا دروازہ بند کرے گا تو اللہ آسمان کے دروازے اس کے لئے بند کریں گے اور اس کی ضرورت اور مسکنت پوری نہیں کریں گے۔
Apparently in the mosque [2899](12) And he should not accept a gift except from a close relative who is mahram to him or from those [the Prophet] judged the adultery case in the mosque. (This is garbled. The meaning of the Urdu commentary is:) There is also a risk of bribery in gifts, that the gift may lead to a wrong judgment. Commentary
This passage provides a ruling on when a judge may accept gifts. The Urdu commentary explains the rationale: gifts can lead to bribery and partial judgments. Therefore, a judge may only accept gifts from two categories: mahram relatives (those whom he cannot marry), and those who were in the habit of giving gifts before his appointment to judgeship. This shows that gifts related to office are problematic. The footnotes at the end are duplicates from the previous lesson and should be ignored.

ذو رحم محرم (dhu rahm mahram): A relative with whom marriage is permanently prohibited, such as mother, sister, daughter.

Ruling in the Mosque for Judges
Hanafi ruling on judging in the mosque with Shafi'i dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a judge sitting in the mosque for adjudication, identify the Shafi'i objection and the Hanafi response, and evaluate the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of the dispute.

Core Idea
A judge may sit in the mosque for adjudication, based on prophetic practice, but must go outside if needed to accommodate those who cannot enter.

This lesson examines a well-known ikhtilaf between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the permissibility of a judge adjudicating cases inside a mosque. The Hanafi school holds it is permissible based on direct prophetic practice, while Imam al-Shafi'i argues that it would exclude polytheists and menstruating women who cannot enter the mosque. Understanding this dispute reveals how each school balances textual evidence (Sunnah) with considerations of accessibility and purity.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafis rule that a judge may sit in the mosque to adjudicate cases. This is based on the Prophet's own practice as recorded in authentic hadiths.
The ruling is one of permissibility (jaiz), not obligation.
2
Identify the opposing school's argument
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that adjudicating in the mosque is disliked (makruh) because it would require polytheists (who are najis) and menstruating/postpartum women to enter the mosque, which is forbidden.
The Shafi'i concern is the preservation of the mosque's sanctity and purity.
3
Examine the Hanafi dalil
The Hanafis cite two hadiths: the man confessing zina in the mosque (Bukhari, Muslim) and the li'an incident in the mosque (Bukhari). They also cite the hadith on not closing doors (Tirmidhi) to emphasize accessibility.
These are sahih ahadith, but they are ahad (zanni al-thubut) in transmission.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafis concede the Shafi'i concern but argue that the judge should go outside to a public place when such persons come, balancing prophetic practice with accessibility.
This shows that the Hanafi ruling is not absolute; it accommodates practical needs.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafi judges may use mosques for adjudication; Shafi'i judges avoid it. Both schools aim for justice, but differ on the venue.
The Hanafi view allows flexibility, while the Shafi'i view prioritizes mosque purity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کتاب آداب القاضی) ظاهرا في المسجد [۲۸۹۹] (۱۲) ولا يقبل هدية الا من ذى رحم محرم منه او ممن حضور نے زنا کا فیصلہ مسجد میں کیا ہے۔ جس سے معلوم ہوا کہ فیصلے کے لئے مسجد میں بیٹھ سکتا ہے۔ حدیث یہ ہے۔ عن ابی ھریرة قال: اتـي رجـل رسول الله الا الله وهو في المسجد فناداه فقال : يا رسول الله ! انی زنیت فاعرض عنه الخ (الف) ( بخاری شریف، باب من حکم فی المسجد الخ، ص ۱۰۶۲، نمبر ۷۱۶۷ر مسلم شریف، باب من اعترف على نفسه بالزنی ص ۶۶ نمبر ۱۹۹۲) (۲) دوسری حدیث میں ہے۔ عن سهل اخی بنی ساعدة ان رجلا من الانصار جاء الى النبي الله فقال ارأيت رجلا وجد مع امرأته رجلا ايقتله ؟ فتلاعنا في المسجد وانا شاهد (ب) ( بخاری شریف، باب من قضی ولائن فی المسجد، ص ۱۰۶۲، نمبر ۷۱۶۶ ) ان احادیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ مسجد میں فیصلہ کیا جا سکتا ہے۔ امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ مسجد میں فیصلے کے لئے نہ بیٹھے۔ وہاں فیصلے کے لئے مشرک آئیں گے جو نجس ہیں وہ مسجد میں کیسے داخل ہوں گے۔ حائضہ اور نفساء عورتیں فیصلے کے لئے آئیں گی جو مسجد میں داخل نہیں ہو سکتیں۔ اس لئے مسجد میں فیصلے کے لئے نہ بیٹھے۔ ہم کہتے ہیں کہ حدیث ہے اس لئے بیٹھ سکتے ہیں البتہ ایسے لوگوں کے لئے قاضی با ہر آجائے۔ اور ایسی جگہ بیٹھے جہاں ہر آدمی آسانی سے فیصلے کے لئے پہنچ سکے اس کی دلیل یہ حدیث ہے۔ قال عمرو بن مرة لمعاوية اني سمعت رسول الله الله يقول : ما من امام يغلق بابه دون ذوى الحاجة والخلة والمسكنة الا اغلق الله ابواب السماء دون خلته وحاجته و مسکنته (ج) ( ترمذی شریف، باب ماجاء فی امام الرعیة، ص ۱۲۴۸، نمبر ۱۳۳۲) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ فیصلے چاہنے والوں کے لئے ظاہر مقام پر بیٹھے تا کہ فیصلہ چاہنے میں رکاوٹ نہ ہو۔

He (the Prophet) gave a ruling on a case of zina in the mosque. From this it is understood that one may sit in the mosque for judgment. The hadith is: From Abu Hurairah, he said: A man came to the Messenger of Allah ﷺ while he was in the mosque and called out: O Messenger of Allah! I committed zina, so he turned away from him, etc. (Bukhari, Muslim). Another hadith: From Sahl, the brother of the Banu Sa'idah, that a man from the Ansar came to the Prophet ﷺ and said: Do you see a man who finds another man with his wife, should he kill him? So they performed li'an in the mosque while I was a witness (Bukhari). From these hadiths it is understood that judgment can be made in the mosque. Imam al-Shafi'i says: One should not sit in the mosque for judgment. There (in the mosque) polytheists who are impure will come for judgment, how can they enter the mosque? Menstruating and postpartum women will come for judgment, who cannot enter the mosque. Therefore, one should not sit in the mosque for judgment. We say: Hadith is evidence, so one may sit. However, for such people, the judge should come outside. And he should sit in a place where every person can easily reach for judgment. The evidence for this is the hadith: 'Amr ibn Murrah said to Mu'awiyah: I heard the Messenger of Allah ﷺ say: There is no ruler who closes his door to those in need, poverty, and misfortune, except that Allah closes the doors of heaven to his need, poverty, and misfortune (Tirmidhi). From this hadith it is understood that he should sit in an open place for those seeking judgment so that there is no obstacle in seeking judgment.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling of a judge sitting in the mosque to adjudicate cases. The Hanafi position is that it is permissible, based on two hadiths: the case of the man confessing zina in the mosque, and the case of li'an performed in the mosque. Imam al-Shafi'i objects, arguing that polytheists (who are ritually impure) and menstruating/postpartum women cannot enter the mosque, so adjudicating there would exclude them. The Hanafi response is that the judge may sit in the mosque when it is practical, but if such persons come, the judge should go outside or to a public place. This is supported by the hadith about the ruler who closes his door, emphasizing accessibility. The passage also introduces the next rule (no gifts except from close relatives) which is part of the same chapter.

Key Terms
مسجد (masjid): The mosque as a place for judging cases, with specific rulings on who can enter.
فیصلہ (faisala): Judgment or adjudication in legal disputes.
نجس (najis): Ritual impurity, here applied to polytheists according to the Shafi'i argument.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on a judge sitting in the mosque to adjudicate cases?
A) It is prohibited due to impurity issues.
B) It is permissible, based on prophetic practice.
C) It is permissible only for criminal cases.
D) It is recommended but not required.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits a judge to sit in the mosque based on prophetic practice, but requires accessibility for all, including those who cannot enter the mosque.

Gifts and Invitations for the Judge
Ruling with proof on judge's gifts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on which gifts a judge may accept and identify the proof from hadith.

Core Idea
A judge may only accept gifts from close relatives or prior habitual givers, as any other gift risks bribery.

Judicial integrity is paramount in Islamic law. Even the appearance of impropriety must be avoided. The Hanafi school provides clear, nuanced guidelines for judges regarding gifts: they are generally prohibited, with two specific exceptions. This lesson unpacks those exceptions, their underlying rationale, and the prophetic evidence that supports them.

Guided Path
1
State the general ruling
Identify the default position: a judge should not accept gifts. This is the starting point of the passage.
The word 'لا يقبل' (la yaqbal) indicates prohibition or strict avoidance.
2
Identify the two exceptions
List the two categories from whom a gift is permissible: (1) close blood relatives (mahram), and (2) those who had a habit of giving gifts before the judge's appointment.
The text uses 'ذی رحم محرم' (close relative) and 'جن کی قاضی بننے سے پہلے ہدیہ دینے کی عادت تھی' (habitual giver).
3
Note the overriding condition
Even from these two categories, the gift is permissible only if it is not given with the intention of bribery. The judge must be vigilant.
The text says 'اگر ایسا ہو تو ان کا ہدیہ بھی قبول نہ کرے' (if it is so, do not accept).
4
Examine the dalil
The hadith of Ibn al-Utbiyyah is cited: a governor was appointed and upon return he said 'This is for you, and this is a gift for me.' The Prophet disapproved, showing that gifts received due to office are not permissible.
The hadith is from Bukhari, Abu Dawud, Tirmidhi. It establishes that official position creates suspicion.
5
Derive the legal implication
The ruling is based on the principle of avoiding bribery (rishwah). Any gift that could be suspected as a bribe is haram, even if from a relative, if intent is corrupt.
The hadith cursing the bribe giver and taker reinforces the severity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۱۲۸۹۹ (۱۲) اور ہدیہ قبول نہ کرے مگر ذی رحم محرم سے یا جن کی قاضی بننے سے پہلے ہدیہ دینے کی عادت تھی۔ ہدیہ میں رشوت کا بھی خطرہ ہے کہ ہدیہ دیگر غلط فیصلہ نہ کر والے۔ اس لئے قاضی ذی رحم محرم سے ہدیہ قبول کرے یا قاضی بنے سے پہلے جن لوگوں کی عادت تھی کہ وہ ہدیہ دیا کرتے تھے انہیں لوگوں سے ہدیہ قبول کرے۔ اور اس کا بھی خیال رکھے کہ وہ لوگ بھی کہیں رشوت کے لئے ہدیہ نہیں دے رہے ہوں۔ اگر ایسا ہو تو ان کا ہدیہ بھی قبول نہ کرے۔ ایسا ہدیہ جس میں رشوت کا شبہ ہو اس کو لینے سے حدیث میں منع فرمایا ہے۔ اخبرنا ابو حميد الساعدي قال استعمل النبي رجلا من بنی اسد يقال له ابن الاتبية على صدقة . فلما قدم قال : هذا لكم وهذاا هدى لى فقام النبي الله على حاشیہ : (الف) ایک آدمی حضور کے پاس آیا ، آپ مسجد میں تشریف رکھتے تھے ۔ اس نے آواز دے کر کہا یا رسول اللہ میں نے زنا کیا، پس آپ نے منہ پھیر لیا (ب) انصار کا ایک آدمی حضور کے پاس آیا اور پوچھا کہ کوئی آدمی اپنی بیوی کے ساتھ کسی کو دیکھے تو اس کوقتل نہ کر دے؟ پھر میاں بیوی دونوں نے مسجد میں لعان کیا اور میں وہاں حاضر تھا (ج) حضور فرمایا کرتے تھے کوئی امام ضرورت والے اور مسکین پر اپنا دروازہ بند کرے گا تو اللہ آسمان کے دروازے اس کے لئے بند کریں گے اور اس کی ضرورت اور مسکنت پوری نہیں کریں گے۔
[12899] (12) And he shall not accept a gift except from a close blood relative (mahram) or from one who was accustomed to giving him gifts before becoming a judge. There is also the danger of bribery in gifts, that a gift may influence a wrong judgment. Therefore, the judge should accept a gift from a close blood relative or from those who were accustomed to giving gifts before he became a judge. And he should also consider whether these people might be giving a gift for bribery. If so, he should not accept their gift. The hadith prohibits taking a gift in which there is suspicion of bribery. Abu Hamid al-Sa'idi reported: The Prophet appointed a man from Banu Asad called Ibn al-Utbiyyah over alms. When he returned, he said: This is for you and this is a gift for me. Then the Prophet stood up... (Hadith continues in footnotes).
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage provides conduct rules for a judge regarding accepting gifts. The general principle is that a judge should not accept gifts from anyone except two categories: (1) close blood relatives (mahram) – because their gifts are not suspected to be bribes, and (2) those who had the habit of giving gifts before the judgeship – because that is normal social interaction, not bribery. However, the judge must be vigilant: if even those people are giving with the intention of influencing judgment, the gift is forbidden. The proof is the hadith of Ibn al-Utbiyyah, where the Prophet criticized a governor who kept gifts for himself, implying that gifts given due to official position are not permissible. Another hadith curses the bribe-giver and bribe-taker, establishing the prohibition of bribery. But gifts from relatives or habitual gift-givers are permissible as long as no bribery intent is present, as supported by the practice of the Prophet accepting gifts even though he was a leader. However, the judge should still be cautious.

Key Terms
ہدیہ (hadiyah): A gift given to a judge, which is prohibited if it is suspected to be a bribe, but permissible from close relatives or habitual givers.
رشوت (rishwah): Bribery, which is haram for both giver and receiver, and cursed in hadith.
دعوت (da'wah): Invitation to a meal; a judge should not attend a private invitation (for fear of influence), but may attend a general public invitation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Any Muslim who gives a gift.
B) Only from close blood relatives (mahram) and those who used to give gifts before judgeship.
C) Only from family members, not others.
D) He may accept from anyone as long as he does not ask for it.

Key Takeaway
A judge must avoid any gift that could be perceived as a bribe; permissible gifts are limited to those from mahram or those with a prior habit, and even then the judge must be vigilant.

Judge's Attendance at Invitations: Private vs General
Ruling on judge avoiding private invitations to prevent bias

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a judge attending private and general invitations and apply the distinction using the proofs from hadith.

Core Idea
A judge may attend general invitations but must avoid private ones to prevent bias.

This lesson addresses the etiquette of a judge regarding social invitations. The core principle is that a judge must avoid any situation that could create suspicion of bias, including private dinner invitations. However, general invitations open to the public, such as wedding feasts, are permissible because the risk of bias is minimized. The ruling is supported by several hadiths that caution against gifts and bribery and encourage accepting general invitations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? It is a judge attending an invitation. The ruling is that it is makruh tanzih (discouraged) for a judge to attend a private invitation, but permissible (mubah) to attend a general invitation.
The text uses 'لا يحضر دعوة' — 'he should not attend an invitation'.
2
Locate the dalil for prohibition of private invitations
The text cites the hadith of the Prophet criticizing an agent who accepted gifts (Bukhari no. 7174) and the hadith cursing the bribe-giver and taker (Abu Dawud no. 3580; Tirmidhi no. 1336). These indicate that any gift or favor that could influence a judge is problematic.
The curse on bribery (al-rashi wal-murtashi) is considered qat'i in meaning, showing strong prohibition.
3
Locate the dalil for permissibility of general invitations
The text cites the hadith of Ibn Umar: 'If any of you is invited to a wedding feast, let him come' (Bukhari no. 5173/5175; Muslim no. 2162). The imperative indicates it is recommended to accept, and for a judge, general invitations are included.
The hadith of al-Bara' ibn 'Azib listing seven commands includes 'accept the invitation', further supporting general invitations.
4
Note the distinction between private and general
The key distinction is whether the invitation is specifically for the judge (private) or open to many (general). Private invitations carry a risk of bias or bribery, so they are avoided. General invitations, like wedding feasts, do not have this risk because they are not targeted.
The text mentions that if food is cooked specifically for the judge, it is likely to incline him.
5
Apply the Hanafi position
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is that a judge should avoid all private invitations but may attend general ones. This is based on the principle of avoiding suspicion (sadd al-dhara'i') and the general sunnah of accepting marriage feasts.
This ruling is part of the broader Hanafi emphasis on a judge's impartiality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جرت عادته قبل القضاء بمهاداته [ ۲۹۰۰] (۱۳) ولا يحضر دعوة الا ان تكون عامة المنبر ... ثم قال: ما بال العامل نبعثه فيأتي فيقول هذا لك وهذا لي؟ فهلا جلس في بيت ابيه وامه فينظر ايهدى له ام لا؟ الخ (الف) ( بخاری شریف، باب هدايا العمال ص ۱۰۶۴، نمبر ۷۱۷۴) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ عمال اور قاضیوں کے لئے بے وقت ہدیہ لینا اچھا نہیں ہے۔ اور رشوت کے طور پر لے تو حرام ہے اس کی دلیل یہ حدیث ہے۔ عن عبد الله بن عمرو قال لعن رسول الله له الراشي والمرتشي (ب) (ابوداؤد شریف ، باب فی کراہیتہ الرشوة ص ۱۳۸، نمبر ۳۵۸۰ / ترمذی شریف، باب ما جاء فی الراشی والمرتشی فی الحکم جس ۲۴۸، نمبر ۱۳۳۶) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ رشوت لینا حرام ہے۔ اور جہاں رشوت کا خطرہ نہ ہو اس سے ہدیہ قبول کرے اس کی دلیل یہ حدیث ہے۔ عن عائشة ان الناس كانوا يتحرون بهداياهم يوم عائشة يبتغون بها او يبتغون بذلك مرضاة رسول الله (ج) ( بخاری شریف، باب قبول الهدیه، ص ۳۵۰ نمبر ۲۵۷۴) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ قریب والوں سے اور جنگی عادت پہلے سے ہدیہ دینے کی ہے اس کا ہدیہ قبول کیا جاسکتا ہے۔ کیونکہ حضور ہدیہ قبول فرمایا کرتے تھے جب کہ آپ قاضی بھی تھے۔ مهادات : ہدیہ سے مشتق ہے، ہدیہ دینا۔
[ ۲۹۰۰ (۱۳) اور دعوت میں حاضر نہ ہو مگر یہ کہ عام ہو۔
خاص طور پر قاضی صاحب کے لئے ہی دعوت کا کھانا بنایا گیا ہو تو ہوسکتا ہے کہ قاضی صاحب کو اپنی طرف مائل کرنے کے لئے کھانا بنایا ہو۔ اس لئے خاص دعوت میں نہ جائیں۔ البتہ بھی کی عام دعوت ہو اس میں قاضی کی بھی دعوت ہو تو جاسکتا ہے۔ حدیث میں ہے۔ قال البراء بن عازب امرنا النبي الا الله بسبع ونهانا عن سبع، امرنا بعيادة المريض واتباع الجنازة وتشميت العاطس وابرار المقسم ونصر المظلوم وافشاء السلام واجابة الداعی ۔ دوسری حدیث میں ہے۔ عن عبد الله بن عمر ان رسول الله له الا الله قال اذا دعى احدكم الى الوليمة فليأتها (و) ( بخاری شریف، باب حق اجابۃ الوليمة والدعوة ومن اولم سبعتہ ایام دخوہ ص ۷۷۷ نمبر ۵۱۷۳/۵۱۷۵ر مسلم شریف ، باب من حق المسلم المسلم رد السلام، ص ۲۱۳، نمبر ۲۱۶۲) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ عام دعوت جیسے ولیمہ وغیرہ میں قاضی کے لئے شرکت کرنا جائز ہے۔
His habit before being a judge was to give gifts. (13) He should not attend an invitation unless it is general. ... Then he (the Prophet) said: 'What is the matter with the agent we send who comes and says, "This is for you and this is for me"? Why doesn't he sit in his father's and mother's house and see if something is given to him as a gift or not?' etc. (a) (Bukhari, Chapter on Gifts for Workers, p. 1064, no. 7174) From this hadith it is learned that it is not good for workers and judges to take gifts untimely. And if taken as a bribe, it is forbidden. Its proof is this hadith: From Abdullah ibn Amr: The Messenger of God cursed the bribe-giver and the bribe-taker (b) (Abu Dawud, Chapter on Dislike of Bribery, p. 138, no. 3580/Tirmidhi, Chapter on Bribe-giver and Bribe-taker in Judgment, vol. 2, p. 248, no. 1336). From this hadith it is learned that taking bribes is forbidden. And where there is no risk of bribery, he may accept gifts. Its proof is this hadith: From Aisha that people would present gifts on Aisha's day, seeking thereby the pleasure of the Messenger of God (c) (Bukhari, Chapter on Accepting Gifts, p. 350, no. 2574). From this hadith it is learned that from relatives and those whose custom it was to give gifts beforehand, their gift may be accepted, because the Prophet used to accept gifts while he was also a judge. Muhadat: derived from gift-giving. [2900] (13) And he should not attend an invitation unless it is general. If food is cooked specifically for the judge, it is possible it is cooked to incline the judge towards them. Therefore, he should not go to a private invitation. However, if there is a general invitation and the judge is also invited, he may go. The hadith says: Al-Bara' ibn 'Azib said: The Prophet commanded us to do seven things and forbade us from seven; he commanded us to visit the sick, follow the funeral, respond to the sneezer, fulfill oaths, help the oppressed, spread salams, and accept the invitation. Another hadith: From Abdullah ibn Umar that the Messenger of God said: 'If any of you is invited to a wedding feast, let him come' (Bukhari, Chapter on Right to Accept Wedding Feast and Invitation, p. 777, no. 5173/5175; Muslim, Chapter on Rights of a Muslim to Return Salams, p. 213, no. 2162). From this hadith it is learned that it is permissible for a judge to attend general invitations like wedding feasts.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage addresses the etiquette of a judge regarding invitations. It begins by noting that a judge should avoid private invitations because they may create bias or be used as a means of bribery. The ruling is that a judge should not attend an invitation unless it is a general one, such as a wedding feast open to all. The text supports this with hadith: the Prophet criticized an agent who accepted gifts, implying that judges and officials should be cautious. The hadith of the Prophet cursing the giver and taker of bribes establishes the prohibition of bribery. However, gifts from relatives or those with a prior custom of giving are permissible if there is no risk of bribery, as the Prophet himself accepted gifts while serving as a judge. The key term 'muhadat' is derived from 'hadiya' (gift). The passage also cites the hadith of al-Bara' ibn 'Azib mentioning acceptance of invitation among seven commands, and the hadith of Ibn Umar specifically about wedding feasts, indicating that general invitations are exempt from the prohibition. Thus, the judge may attend such events.

Key Terms
مهادات (muhādāt): Gift-giving, specifically the exchange of gifts prior to judgment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Ruling for a Judge to Attend Funerals and Visit the Sick
Ruling that a judge must attend funerals and visit the sick

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a judge must attend funerals and visit the sick, and identify the dalil that supports this obligation.

Core Idea
A judge performs social duties like any Muslim; no risk of bias justifies exemption.

This passage addresses a common concern: does a judge's position exempt him from general Muslim social duties? The text clarifies that attending funerals and visiting the sick are communal obligations, safe from bribery, and thus the judge must fulfill them. The ruling is supported by a hadith commanding these actions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a judge attending funerals and visiting the sick. The ruling is that it is a communal obligation (fard kifayah) that the judge must fulfill.
The text says 'judge will do all these'.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith from Bukhari and Muslim: 'He commanded us to visit the sick and follow the funeral.' This is the proof.
The hadith is an ahad hadith, so zanni al-thubut.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
In Hanafi usul, the imperative 'amarna' indicates obligation (wujub). Since the command is general, it creates a fard kifayah on the community, including the judge.
4
Note the specific context for the judge
The text explicitly states there is no risk of bribery in these acts, so the judge's participation does not compromise impartiality.
This eliminates any excuse for exemption.
5
Conclude the ruling
Therefore, the judge must attend funerals and visit the sick like any other Muslim.
The ruling is mutual agreement among schools (muttafaq alayh).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۹۰۱] (۱۴) ويشهد الجنازة ويعود المريض [۲۹۰۱] (۱۴) جنازے میں حاضر ہوا اور بیمار کی عیادت کرے۔ جنازے میں شرکت کرنے اور بیمار کی عیادت کرنے میں رشوت کا خطرہ نہیں ہے بلکہ یہ انسانی حقوق ہیں اور حدیث کے اعتبار سے ضروری ہیں اس لئے یہ سب قاضی کریں گے۔ او پر حدیث گزرگئی۔ امرنا بعيادة المريض واتباع الجنازة (بخاری شریف نمبر ۵۱۷۵ر مسلم شریف ۲۱۶۲)

Therefore, the judge will do all these. The hadith has passed earlier: 'He commanded us to visit the sick and follow the funeral' (Bukhari no. 5175, Muslim no. 2162).

Commentary
This passage states a ruling regarding a judge's social obligations. The judge is required to attend funerals and visit the sick, as these actions do not involve any risk of bribery or partiality. Rather, they are fundamental human rights and Islamic duties. The text references the previously mentioned hadith from al-Bara' ibn 'Azib where the Prophet commanded seven things, including visiting the sick and following funerals. The judge is not exempt from these communal obligations.

Key Terms
عيادة المريض (iyādat al-marīḍ): Visiting the sick as a social and religious duty.
